Authors: Freddy Johannes Madsen / Thomas Raahauge Lund Nielsen 
Title: Experimental and numerical study of the scaled DTU 10MW floating 
wind turbine on a TLP platform 
 
DTU Wind Energy-M-0162 
August 2017 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
DTU Wind Energy is a department of the Technical University of Denmark 
with a unique integration of research, education, innovation and public/private 
sector consulting in the field of wind energy. Our activities develop new 
opportunities and technology for the global and Danish exploitation of wind 
energy. Research focuses on key technical-scientific fields, which are central 
for the development, innovation and use of wind energy and provides the 
basis for advanced education at the education. 
 
We have more than 240 staff members of which approximately 60 are PhD 
students. Research is conducted within nine research programmes organized 
into three main topics: Wind energy systems, Wind turbine technology and 
Basics for wind energy. 
Project Period:  
January – August 2017 
 
ECTS: 35 
 
Education: Master of Science 
 
 
Supervisors: 
 
Taeseong Kim 
Henrik Bredmose 
Michael Borg 
DTU Wind Energy 
 
 
Remarks: 
This report is submitted as partial 
fulfillment of the requirements for 
graduation in the above education at the 
Technical University of Denmark.

==PAGE:3==
Abstract
This master thesis deals with an experimental and numerical investigation of the dynamic response of a
1/60 scale model of the DTU 10MW Reference Wind Turbine on a TLP type ﬂoater. The TLP foundation
was designed and manufactured by the Korean Institute of Energy Research (KIER) based on an extreme
load analysis of the DTU 10MW RWT. The structure and the environmental conditions were scaled using
Froude scaling, while the rotor was performance scaled in order to produce the scaled aerodynamic thrust.
The model was tested for four different environmental conditions including wind and regular, irregular, and
focused waves. Furthermore, including closed-loop control, based on the Basic DTU Wind Energy controller.
The motion in six degrees of freedom, nacelle accelerations, shear force between tower and nacelle, mooring
line tension, and turbine operational data were measured and constitutes the basis of the analysis of the
response.
Numerically the present study applies two different aero-hydro-servo-elastic codes, HAWC2 and FAST. A
hydrodynamic panel model in WAMIT was set up to provide the aero-elastic codes with diffraction and
radiation wave forcing on the ﬂoater. Viscous drag terms are applied separately through strip theory in both
codes. The two models were calibrated by matching key parameters; steady thrust, decay tests and response
to regular waves with and without wind.
In the experimental study it was found that aligned wind and waves result in damped in-line motion. This
effect is smaller when an oblique wave heading angle is introduced than for co-aligned wind and waves. An
increase in transverse and yaw motion was observed while the in-line motions were reduced slightly subject
to spread waves compared to long crested waves. Furthermore, the correct control strategy showed decisive
in order to avoid excessive structural response. Based on a phase-separation approach, it was seen that surge
motion was subject to a signiﬁcant amount of higher order forcing.
In the numerical study, a good agreement between FAST and HAWC2 predictions is seen despite that they
deviate for the largest motions. An acceptable agreement between models and measurements were present
for in-line motions in the most severe sea states. Yet, for the less severe cases, both models underpredicted
the response in irregular waves. Indicators as to why the differences occur are elaborated on. Transverse
motions were not possible to match due to asymmetry in the alignment of the ﬂoater. The implementation of
active control showed good comparison to data. The numerical models proved capable of capturing the the
transient dynamics of extreme focused waves.

Acknowledgements
This master thesis was carried out at the Wind Energy department at the Danish Technical University of
Denmark by Freddy J. Madsen and Thomas R. L. Nielsen. The project was carried out from January 2017 to
August 2017, with one month break in June due to employment in a test campaign involving DTU Wind
Energy, Stiesdal A/S and The Danish Hydraulic Institute (DHI). Also in June, we presented two abstracts at
the Wind Energy Science Conference 2017 based on the experimental and numerical work carried out for
this master thesis.
First of all we would like to dedicate a special thank you to Pyungho Shin on behalf of the Korea Institute of
Energy Research for founding the project and thereby making this master thesis possible. The experimental
work done in this project involved many people from both DTU Wind Energy and DHI. Without their help
it would not have been possible. A big thanks to the DTU team of the KIER-DTU 2016 test campaign
which besides ourself consisted of MSc. Anders K. Lomholt, PhD. Antonio M. P. Jurado, MSc. Esma
Mujkic, Associate Professor Henrik Bredmose, Researcher Mahmood Miraei, Postdoc Michael Borg, Senior
Researcher Robert F. Mikkelsen and project manager Associate Professor Taeseong Kim. A good exchange
of experiences and expertises, especially took especially place during great lunches in the canteen of DHI.
Continuing, we would like to thank DHI for letting us use their facilities and we are particularly grateful for
the guidance from hydraulic engineer Bjarne Jensen and technician Poul Hansen during the test campaign.
Special thanks to our great supervisors Taeseong Kim, Henrik Bredmose and Michael Borg. Henrik, for
his enthusiastic approach and enormous knowledge of hydrodynamics and Taeseong for valuable insight in
structural dynamics and to encourage us to take part in WESC2017. We sincerely appreciate our valuable
discussions. The support from Michael Borg we are very grateful of and his ability to withstand our inﬁnite
number of questions has been remarkable. Also Robert F. Mikkelsen deserves our gratitude for his great
knowledge of experimental setup and his good mood.
Many people were also involved in the numerical part of the project and here we would like to thank chief
engineer Anders Melchior Hansen for his invaluable insight in HAWC2 and devoting time for debugging and
adjusting the source codes. Professor Harry Bingham for providing access to MultiSurf and tips on WAMIT
modelling. Another big thanks is devoted to PhD student Antonio M.P. Jurado for his sublime support
regarding FAST. We are also very grateful for the work done by researcher Mahmood Mirzai regarding the
model controller.
Lastly special thanks goes to our families and girlfriends for their unconditional love and support.
Freddy J. Madsen & Thomas R.L. Nielsen
DTU, Kongens Lyngby, August 2017

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
Over the past 40 years, wind energy has evolved from a small industry active in only a few countries
to a large international industry involving major players in the manufacturing, development, and
utility sectors [1]. The rapid industry growth and a signiﬁcant innovation in wind turbine technology
have resulted in large sized turbines with reduced Levelised Cost of Energy and more complex
designs in all subsystems. However, installing large turbines onshore can be difﬁcult, due to visual
issues and noise legislation, road restrictions and high terrain roughness’ which complicates the
erection of turbines here. This is why wind turbines are also being installed offshore in seas, bays
and lakes.
The offshore wind industry started in 1991 when the ﬁrst offshore wind farm was installed in
Vindeby (Denmark) with a park capacity of 4.95 MW spread over 11 turbines. However, it was
not until 2001 where the next offshore wind farm was constructed in Middelgrunden,Denmark,
with a capacity of 40 MW, that the market started to grow [2]. Only a year later in, 2002, the ﬁrst
large-scale offshore wind farm Horns Rev I,Denmark, was installed with an impressive capacity of
160 MW, four times larger than Middelgrunden.
The last decade has seen a steady growth in the offshore wind industry both in terms of installations,
capacity and in intense R&D. This can be seen on Figure 1.1. As in end-2015, the cumulative
installed offshore capacity reached 11 GW spread across 80 offshore wind farms in 11 European
countries.
Most offshore wind turbines are installed in shallow water on bottom-ﬁxed foundations such as
monopiles and lattice frames, i.e. jackets and tripods. The offshore wind energy market is one
of the fastest growing maritime sectors [3] attracting the attention of wind energy companies,
developing wind farms in shallow waters mainly found in the Baltic Sea, the North Sea and the
coasts of England. In the United States, China, Japan, Spain, Norway, and many other countries,
the shallow water availability is very limited and together with space requirements for onshore
wind turbines and public pressure to place wind turbines out of visual range, it push as the demand
for offshore wind farms in deeper waters.
1
==PAGE:22==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
Figure 1.1: Europe’s cumulative and annual offshore wind growth. Source: EWEA [4]
This is why ﬂoating wind turbine concepts have gathered increasing interest, though, as offshore
wind farms tend to move towards deeper waters it stretches the limits for bottom-ﬁxed foundations.
The foundations are the main challenge in the offshore wind industry as the cost of the foundation
increases heavily as the water depth increases. Figure 1.2 shows the average water depth and
distance to shore, for consented- and wind farms under construction by representing the total
capacity of the wind farm. It can be seen that the wind farms are moving further away from shore
and towards deeper waters, reaching was seems to be the current maximum water depth of 50 m.
In order to unlock market potential for water depths of above 50 m, researchers and companies are
now investigating the concept of ﬂoating support platforms which must have to be introduced to
make deep water windfarms more feasible economically [5].
Figure 1.2: Average water depth and distance to shore of active, under construction and consented
wind farms as of February 2016. Source: EWEA [3]
2
==PAGE:23==
1.1
Floating Wind Turbine Concepts
The concept of ﬂoating support platform conﬁgurations has been used in the offshore oil and gas
industries but it is quite new for the wind industry [6]. Based on the variety of mooring systems,
tanks, and ballast options used in the oil and gas industries, several concepts for offshore wind
turbines has been adapted. There are three primary ﬂoating platform classes, which are classiﬁed in
terms of how the designs achieve static stability. A schematic drawing of the three different types
of static stability methods are shown in Figure 1.3(a). A ﬂoating support platform can be stabilised
by buoyancy, ballast and mooring, see Figure 1.3(b).
(a)
(b)
Figure 1.3: Left: Floating support platform stability triangle. Source [7]. Right: the three main
ﬂoating wind turbine concepts; spar buoy, tension leg platform (TLP) and barge. Source: [8]
Each of the concepts has its own advantages and disadvantages and is is still an open question which
ﬂoater concept is the most economically feasible. Many concepts exist on different development
stages, however, only a few are mentioned here.
• The spar buoy concept consists of a long vertical cylinder platform and it achieves stability
by using ballast in the cylinder to lower the centre of gravity (CoG) below the centre of
buoyancy (CoB). The structure can either be moored by catenary or taut lines. Example:
Hywind, Statoil.
• The tension leg platform (TLP) achieves stability through the use of the mooring line tension
by excess buoyancy of the platform. Since the structure is having positive buoyancy, the
additional buoyancy needs to be balanced by applying equal pretension in the mooring lines
to achieve stability. Example: PelaStar by Glosten.
• In the barge concept, the barge is generally moored by catenary lines and achieve stability
through the use of distributed buoyancy, taking advantage of weighted water plane area for
righting moment. Example: Damping pool by IDEOL.
3
==PAGE:24==
Related Studies
However concepts with features from all three classes, called hybrid concepts, are also a possibility,
such as the semi-submersible ﬂoater. This structure is often made of three or four interconnected
cylinders each anchored with catenary mooring lines. It achieves its stabilization through a combi-
nation of ballast and buoyancy. An example is WindFloat by Principle Power.
1.2
Related Studies
Only a handful of ﬂoating wind turbines exist worldwide, but an increasing interest for ﬂoating
wind turbines in the industry have increased over the recent decade. This interest from the industry’s
side reﬂects in the academic world where numerous of numerically and experimentally test have
been conducted on different concepts of ﬂoating concepts. The increased academic research then
helps pushing the offshore industry forward.
The Danish Technical University of Denmark (DTU) has been involved in a lot of research re-
garding ﬂoating wind turbines both theoretically and experimentally. Especially the amount of
conducted and planned model experiments has been growing a lot over the last 5 years. Even a
summer school course at DTU has just been created and is running for the ﬁrst time in August 2017.
It takes the participants through the design phase of ﬂoating wind turbines based on numerical
models, the manufacturing process and ending up in actual small model testing of the design with
wind and waves.
In 2012 the ﬁrst experimental setup with a ﬂoating wind turbine at DTU was the test campaign of a
1/200 scale model of the NREL 5MW reference wind turbine on a tension leg platform support
structure investigating the response in wind and waves simultaneously. The experiments were
carried out by, at that time BSc students, R. Laugesen and A. M. Hansen, in DTU’s own small scale
wave basin. This study was the ﬁrst to report model tests of a ﬂoating wind turbine in combined
wind and waves with a redesigned rotor for the low Reynolds numbers [9].
This study led to the next very ambitious experimental study in 2015 of a 1/60 model of the DTU
10 MW reference wind turbine on a tension leg platform [9]. The study was carried out by the same,
now MSc. students, R. Laugesen and A. M. Hansen and in close collaboration with the Danish
Hydraulic Institute (DHI) where the experiments were conducted. The study included experimental
analysis in both wind and waves. The full scale turbine was Froude scaled and the blades was
scaled based on aerodynamic performance. This resulted in a rotor diameter of 3 m and a hub
height of 2 m. This TLP campaign has provided an experimental insight of the dynamic response
in different environmental conditions and a lot of data for which could be used for validating
numerical models. Experimental ﬁndings published in [10], [11] and numerical assessment in i.e.
[12]. This led to two numerical investigations in the form of two MSc projects being conducted
in 2015. A. S. Kocaturk and A. M. P. Jurado implemented each a numerical in HAWC2 [13] and
FLEX5 [14] respectively. The latter based on work of [15]
4
==PAGE:25==
In 2016 the next test campaign was conducted investigating a new ﬂoating support structure, i.e. the
triple spar concept. The study was carried out by two MSc students T. Fjelstrup and T. S. Larsen
[16] with support from two BSc students A. K. Lomholt and L. Boehm [17]. The campaign was a
part of the INNWIND.EU project in collaboration with University of Stuttgart and CENER. Once
again, the tests were carried out at DHI. Measurements were carried out with the same 1/60 model
of the DTU 10MW reference wind turbine as in the TLP campaign. The INNWIND.EU Triple
Spar Floater introduced active wind turbine control as opposed to open-loop control in [9].
1.3
Project Scope
The design of the support structures for ﬂoating wind turbines relies heavily on numerical modelling
and while several coupled models exist, data sets for their validation are scarce. Validation, however,
is important due to the complexity of the overall turbine behaviour in aero- and hydrodynamic
loads, mooring loads and turbine control. The validation of numerical models for ﬂoating wind
turbines plays an important role in the design phase of ﬂoating support structures, both adding
reliability to the design tools and conﬁdence to the sector of ﬂoating wind turbines, but also ul-
timately pushing the offshore industry forward, thus bringing down the cost of offshore wind energy.
The main goals of this master thesis will be to carry out a detailed analysis of the obtained data
and compare the experimental results with numerical simulations. The numerical model of the
tested ﬂoating wind turbine will be set up in two aero-hydro-elastic codes; FAST and HAWC2.
The construction of the two models includes implementation of the DTU controller used in the
experiment, subsequently tuned and validated. In order to describe the hydrodynamic properties of
the ﬂoater in the two aero-hydro-elastic codes a WAMIT model will be conducted.
The dynamic response of the system, with a view to the 6DOF ﬂoater response, the mooring
line tensions and the nacelle acceleration, will be investigated in different environmental con-
ditions for regular, irregular and focused waves both with and without wind. The wave cases
are divided into two cases i.e. aligned and 30◦misaligned with respect to the wind direction.
The inﬂuence of second order waves will be analysed based on test runs carried out with inverted
wave signals. In addition, effects of spread waves (3D) in the measurement data will be investigated.
Furthermore, the difference in having a land based controller and offshore controller will be
investigated in the different environmental conditions, but also an advanced analysis of the response
due to shutdown effects will be conducted. In the end the two numerical models are validated
against the experimental data and each other.
5
==PAGE:26==
==PAGE:27==
CHAPTER 2
The DTU-KIER TLP Wind Turbine
Model Concept
This chapter will present the scaled TLP wind turbine model. The global coordinate system used
throughout this thesis will be described and the corresponding degrees of freedom will be deﬁned.
The loads acting on a ﬂoating wind turbine will be discussed and the theory of how to model the
main loads will be described. In addition, the implementation of closed-loop control presented.
2.1
Coordinate System
A TLP consists of a ﬂoater body with a more slender transition piece on top and outgoing spokes
mounted on the ﬂoater body, where the taut mooring lines are attached. The total buoyancy is
larger than the total weight of the structure, causing excess buoyancy which yields the tension in
the mooring lines. The wind turbine is mounted on top of the transition piece.
The ﬂoating system is exposed to translations and rotations motion due to the wind and wave loads.
The global coordinate system, as seen in Figure 2.1 has its origin at z = 0, which is located at the
middle of the transition piece, where the mean water level is. Surge and sway are the horizontal
motions along x and y axes respectively, whereas the vertical motion, heave, runs along the z-axis
through the centerline of the structure. The rotational motions about x, y and z axes are represented
as pitch, roll and yaw respectively.
7
==PAGE:28==
Equation of Motion
Figure 2.1: The global coordinate system of the TLP Wind Turbine
2.2
Equation of Motion
In order to visualise the combined dynamics of the turbine and ﬂoater, a simpliﬁed equation of
motion is shown in (2.1). Hence this is not an attempt to reproduce the theory behind FAST and
HAWC2, rather to give a feeling of the different terms. The forces acting on the combined system
can for simplicity be divided into three components in addition to the forces exerted by gravity, ⃗FG,
1) Aerodynamic loads from wind 2) Hydrodynamic loads from incident waves and buoyancy and 3)
Forces from mooring lines. All of which consists of three translational forces and three moments.
and will be introduced in the next section.
(M + A)⃗¨ξ + B⃗˙ξ + C⃗ξ = ⃗FG + ⃗FWind + ⃗FHydro + ⃗FMoor
(2.1)
where ⃗ξ = [ξ1, ξ2, ..., ξ6]T correspond to the system six degree of freedom, i.e the translational
degrees of freedom; surge, sway, heave and the rotational degrees of freedom; roll, pitch and
yaw. Here M illustrates the mass matrix of the combined system of ﬂoater and turbine.A is a
8
==PAGE:29==
resulting additional "mass" originating from the submerged structure moving in water. B denotes
the damping matrix and C the stiffness, or restoring matrix, ensuring that the structure seek to
return to equilibrium.
2.3
Loads on Offshore Wind Turbines
Wind turbines on ﬂoating support platforms are designed to be installed in deep water offshore
environments located a great distance from the coast and in water depths greater than 50 m.
These systems are exposed to a series of loads which inﬂuences the dynamic response of the
structure, as seen in Figure 2.2.The response is dominated by aerodynamic and hydrodynamic
effects. The effects from icing, sea ice, varying mean water level (MWL), and marine growth
constitute additional loads that must considered, when relevant, in a real design process. The
following section presents an overview of the wind and wave loads acting on a ﬂoating wind turbine
and how they are modelled for this project.
Figure 2.2: Loads on an offshore wind turbine [6]
2.3.1
Aerodynamic Loading
The steady Blade Element Momentum (BEM) theory is widely used to estimate the annual energy
production of a wind turbine by calculating the power and thrust based on the assumption of a
stiff turbine and steady wind. However, a wind turbine is subject to wind loads, which are time
9
==PAGE:30==
Loads on Offshore Wind Turbines
dependent and in order to account for the unsteadiness of the wind caused by wind shear, tower
shadow, dynamics stall effects and turbulence, the unsteady BEM model is introduced [18]. By
coupling the unsteady BEM model with a structural model of the wind turbine, the result is called
an aero-elastic code, where the aerodynamic loads are based on the relative velocity seen by the
blade, which is dependent on the structural velocity, thus a consequence of the aerodynamic loading.
The theory and procedure of the the unsteady BEM model is presented in Appendix B.1, and
describes how several coordinate systems, i.e. one for each main component as seen in Figure 2.3,
are needed in order to transform the motions of the components into the ﬁxed frame of reference
and vice versa. See the Appendix for the coordinate system deﬁnitions. Furthermore, the equation
of the relative wind velocity seen by the blade is written and is one of the key variables in the
unsteady BEM code as it couples the motions originating from the structural-, hydrodynamic- and
aerodynamic part. It is then described how the velocities are converted into the aerodynamic thrust
and torque followed by brief presentation of the implemented corrections and empirical models.
Figure 2.3: A ﬂoating wind turbine described with six coordinate systems.
10
==PAGE:31==
2.3.2
Hydrodynamic Loading
The approach to hydrodynamic loading in this project is a combination of 1) the linear hydrody-
namic forcing, which can be divided into radiation, excitation forces and hydrostatics and secondly
2) non-linear viscous drag originating from incoming waves and platform motion. This approach
was decided optimum in order to model the hydrodynamics of the ﬂoater as will be discussed
in this section. The linear hydrodynamic forces has been calculated in the potential ﬂow-panel
method WAMIT, see Section 5.2. Linear wave theory for regular and irregular waves are shown in
Appendix B.2.
The two most signiﬁcant non-dimensional numbers to describe oscillating ﬂow around offshore
structures are the Reynolds number and the Keulegan-Carpenter number. The Reynolds number
describes the degree of turbulence in a (steady) ﬂow and is computed by the diameter of the
structure, D, the ﬂow velocity U and the kinematic viscosity, ν,
Re = DU
ν
(2.2)
The Keulegan-Carpenter quantiﬁes the relative displacement of water particles relative to the
diameter of the cylinder,
KC = UmTw
D
(2.3)
where Um is the maximum velocity and Tw is the period of oscillating ﬂow, i.e. wave period. The
KC number provides information on how likely it is that ﬂow separation will occur. Small KC
numbers means that the ﬂuid particle travels a short distance compared to the diameter of the
structure. Furthermore, KC < 2 indicates attached ﬂow, i.e. no ﬂow separation, which is required
for the application of potential theory to calculate diffraction forces.
According to DNV Recommended Practice (DNV-RP-C205), a body may be deﬁned as a large
volume structure if the dimensions, D, are of the same magnitude as the typical wavelengths,
L, expressed as D > L/6. This is true for the main ﬂoater body. Thereby diffraction effects,
disturbance of incident wave ﬁeld by the structure, becomes important. Thus, application of only
the well known Morison equation (2.4), is no longer a suitable choice. The Morison equation is
widely used for calculating loads on slender (cylindrical) bodies with a limited range of movement,
i.e. bottom-ﬁxed foundations. However, to allow for a body with limited movement, it is presented
here in its relative form, where Ub & ˙Ub represent the velocity and acceleration of the moving body
respectively. This is also the form implemented in FAST and HAWC2. The ﬁrst term in the equation
describes the non-linear drag force on a body. The second term describes the hydrodynamic mass
force which together with the last term, the Froude-Krylov force, makes up the inertia forces
included in the Morison equation [19].
F = 1
2ρ CDD(U −Ub) |(U −Ub)| + ρ CmA( ˙U −˙Ub) + ρA ˙U
(2.4)
11
==PAGE:32==
Loads on Offshore Wind Turbines
The drag coefﬁcient, CD and the inertia coefﬁcient Cm depend on the surrounding ﬂow, i.e. by Re-
and KC number dependence. The cross sectional area is denoted A.
Low KC numbers, KC << 20 −30, are considered an inertia dominated regime while KC >
20 −30 is considered drag dominated regime [20, ch. 4].This implies that the inertia forces
dominate the in-line forces in this ﬂow regime where little or no separation occurs. However, with
increasing KC numbers, the viscous drag force becomes more important. The viscous drag force
can also play a signiﬁcant role in terms of damping as discussed in [20, ch. 4]. Assuming linear
deep water theory and looking at a wave corresponding to signiﬁcant wave height and period for
EC8, listed in Section 3.1, umax = πH
T
= 0.33 m/s yields KC ≈4 for the transition piece and
KC ≈1 if looking at the main ﬂoater body. This reveals that for the most severe seastate, the ﬂow
might start separating at the uppermost part of the cylinder. However, this is the only location and
potential theory is therefore still generally valid for the KIER-TLP.
Consequently, the ﬁrst term of (2.4) is included in the hydrodynamic modelling for the ﬂoater. The
viscous drag is computed through application of strip theory in the numerical models. Please go to
Section 5.3 for more information on the numerical implementation.
Excitation force
The excitation forces consist of diffraction and the Froude-Krylov force. The Froude-Krylov force
represents the pressure gradient in the outer ﬂow, also known as the undisturbed pressure ﬁeld in
the incoming wave. It can be calculated from the incident wave potential, φi, [20]
p = −ρ∂φ0
∂t
(2.5)
On the other hand, diffraction describes the disturbance which the ﬁxed body causes to the incoming
wave and its pressure ﬁeld. The undisturbed wave and the diffracted wave are in conjunction called
a scattered wave. The inﬂuence of both Froude-Krylov, and diffraction, and how it is calculated
in WAMIT can be found in Section C. However, for completion, the total excitation force in the
direction i, Xi, in the shape of the complex amplitude, is deﬁned in [21] as,
Xi = −ρ

(2.6)
where φD is the diffraction potential and φ0 denotes the undisturbed incoming wave ﬁeld. The
excitation force is independent of the body motions.
Radiation force
Radiation forces consist of added mass and a damping term. They are independent of the incident
waves and rely solely on body motions. The damping term originates from the fact that the moving
body will generate outgoing waves, which have an effect on the ﬂuid pressure ﬁeld. Secondly,
the added mass is the inertia added to a system due to the acceleration of the surrounding ﬂuid

when the body moves. The six components of the radiation force and moment can be written as the

are dependent on the frequency. In addition, it is also heavily dependent on the shape of the body.
Restoring coefﬁcients - Hydrostatics
The contribution to the loads from hydrostatic forces will add to the stiffness of the system, i.e.
a restoring force. This also includes buoyancy forces which will be non-zero only in the case of
vertical displacement; heave. The buoyancy force, FB, is generated due to the displacement of the
surrounding ﬂuid, which creates an upward force. It originates from the Archimedes principle,
FB = ρg∇,
(2.10)
where the volume of displaced water by the submerged body is denoted ∇. FB acts in the Center
of Buoyancy (CB) of the body.
The change in hydrostatic force and moment due to change in water plane area and CB is listed in
(2.11) which shows the only non-zero elements of the 6x6 restoring matrix with a body symmetry
at y = 0. The change in water plane area and location of CB is controlled by the rotation of the
body. Here IA

This section serves as an overview to what parameters inﬂuence the restoring force originating from
the mooring lines and is not a comprehensive derivation of the actual restoring coefﬁcients from

mooring lines. For a TLP, the restoring force from the mooring lines is crucial in order to achieve

position the total tension in the mooring lines are given by,
T0 = FB −FG
(2.12)
where FG = (mturbine + mfloater)g represents the total gravity force. In the following a brief

stiffness. Seeing that the mass of the mooring lines is small compared to the mass of the ﬂoater and
turbine, the inertia and buoyancy forces are neglected as well. Furthermore, the buoyancy change
due to vertical set-down is neglected and for translations small displacement compared to mooring
line length is assumed.

2.4
The TLP Wind Turbine Model
This section will present a description of the scaled TLP wind turbine used in the experiments.
This will include the scaling procedure of the main components and the criteria behind the design.
In the end the key speciﬁcations of the model will be shown. The combined TLP ﬂoating wind
turbine model assembled in the wave basin can be seen in Figure 2.4.
Figure 2.4: The full TLP ﬂoating wind turbine model.
2.4.1
Wind Turbine Design
The DTU 10MW RWT has been been scaled to a 1/60 model scale in previous work [9]. The
method of scaling will be presented in this section. First, key parameters for the full scaled DTU
10MW RWT are shown in Table 2.1. Details of the wind turbine model is described in [25].
The full scaled 10MW RWT model is scaled 1/60 for the measurement. When setting up a model
experiment, the aim is to have dynamic and elastic similarity between the scaled model and its
original model. The motion of a rigid body in a ﬂuid, is generally governed by inertial forces,
gravitational forces and viscous forces, and to obtain full dynamic similarity, the ratio of these

represent a signiﬁcant change in the length scale, which is clearly observed by considering the ratio
of the Reynolds number to the Froude number,
Re
Fr =

gl3

.
(2.19)
As a consequence of the constant Re and Fr, this quantity must also be preserved, requiring either
a substantial change in the acceleration of gravity or the ﬂuid viscosity, but neither are considered
possible for model tests. Surface waves are primarily governed by Fr, and hence it has become
tradition to preserve this number in model tests of offshore structures and ﬂoating bodies, and this
methodology will also serve as a starting point for the scaling of the wind turbine model in the
16
==PAGE:37==

and equations are described in [9].

The rotor aerodynamics are mainly governed by Re, and due to the large dissimilitude in Re, a
geometrical scaling of the rotor will not give the correct aerodynamic performance of the blades.
This will lead to a reduction in the aerodynamic thrust and mechanical power, where especially the
ﬁrst is of great importance to the overall dynamics of the structure. Hence, there is a need for a
redesign of the rotor in model scale, which is able to deliver the right thrust at the low Re. Therefore
it was decided to aim for a thrust-matched rotor design. Moreover effort was put into matching
the tip speed ratio correctly and scaling the mass of the blades. The work of designing the thrust-
matched blades was conducted by Robert Mikkelsen based on aero-servo-elastic computations in
FLEX 5. The overall design criteria for the thrust-matched blades are,
• Tip speed ratio is to be preserved
• The axial blade loading and thrust coefﬁcient should be matched
• The design is based on a full scale wind speed of 10 m/s
• Preserve chord distribution scaled with the same factor for all radii
Figure 2.5 shows the manufactured blade for the scaled turbine model. Details for the blade design
and manufacturing process are described in [9].
17
==PAGE:38==
The TLP Wind Turbine Model
Figure 2.5: Manufactured blade for the scaled wind turbine model
The hub and nacelle of the scaled wind turbine model is much more simple in its composition
compared to a full scale, and the number of components is reduced signiﬁcantly for the scaled
model. The main functions of the hub and nacelle are to contain the motors, for control torque and
blade pitch. The main focus was on limiting the model weight, since the Froude scaled wind only
induces a small drag on the nacelle. The active pitch control system on the model wind turbine
implies added complexity of the wind turbine design. The basic idea in the pitch mechanism for the
model is to convert the rotary motion of the pitch actuator into a translatory motion which again
can be converted into an angular displacement of the blades. The two JVL MAC050 servomotors,
one for torque control and one for blade pitch control, to achieve a faster reacting controller are
used. The properties of the servomotors are shown in 2.3. Figure 2.6 shows the entire hub and
nacelle system. More details about the nacelle and hub system are described in [9]. The control
mechanism and software used in this project are addressed in Section 2.5.2 and 3.4.

2.5
Control System
In this section a brief introduction to control theory of wind turbines will be presented. Furthermore
the type of control mechanisms that were used for the experiments will be described and how they
were tuned.
2.5.1
Control theory
A control system in wind turbines is essential for efﬁcient and reliable power production, but
also to ensure safe operation under all wind conditions. A simpliﬁed version of the Basic DTU
Wind Energy Controller [28] was used in this project, since it was decided only to include start-up,
shut-down and partial and full load operation capabilities in the controller.
The simpliﬁed controller is a variable speed, variable collective pitch controller working in a closed
loop. This means that the controller outputs are computed on basis on the inputs to the controller,
i.e. the rotational speed, measured blade pitch and wind speed. These inputs are ﬁltered, affected by
gains and checked that they are within safety limits. The output of the controller is a pitch reference
setting and a generator torque. The Basic DTU Wind Energy controller is documented in [28].
The objective of the controller is to maximize the power production during the life time of the
wind turbine. This is achieved by maximizing power generation for the low wind speeds, i.e.
under rated conditions, while keeping the rotational speed within the operation limits. Above rated
conditions the objectives of the controller is to ensure the safety and increase the lifetime of the
structure by minimizing loads and variation of the generator torque and keeping operation within
generator speed limits. An example is that the "rate of change" of the generator torque is rather
slow compared to the pitching speed in order to reduce stress on the shaft and drive train.
This means that wind turbines have two distinct regions of operation. The ﬁrst one being the low
wind speed region, below rated conditions, called the partial load region, and second being the
high wind speed region,above rated conditions, called the full load region. Both regions aim to
keep a balance between the generator and aerodynamic torque to obtain the best possible power
coefﬁcient (Cp) by adjusting the Tip Speed Ratio (TSR) or the blade pitch (θp). The two distinct
regions of operation can be seen in Figure 2.8 together with two transition regions (illustrated as
the green regions). The ﬁrst transition region is between the cut-in wind speed and region 1. Here
the rotational speed is constant at the minimum limit Ωmin in order to maximize Cp and the blades
are pitched into the wind. In Region 1 the optimum values for the blade pitch θp,opt and Tip Speed
Ratio λopt are achieved and kept constant. This means that the turbine now has to operate with
variable rotational speed Ωand this is regulated in the controller by the generator reaction torque.
The transition region between region 1 and region 2 is where the turbine starts to produce maximum
power. Therefore, the blade pitch is regulated, while the maximum value of the rotational speed is
reached Ωmax, hence λopt) can not be held either. Region 2 is the full load region with constant
speed and constant power, maintained by the controller by regulating the blade pitch.
21
==PAGE:42==
Control System
Figure 2.8: Different regions of the scaled wind turbine control. Fullscale data courtesy of [25]
The parameters, e.g. the gains, that goes into the controller to optimize performance and minimize
loads are computed by the pole-placement method. A linear method that determines the desired
position of the poles in the closed loop system by setting a gain for the controller. The placement
of the poles are controlling the response of the system.
Partial Load Operation
The control objective for the partial load region is to maximize the generated power. For the ﬁrst
region, i.e. the ﬁrst transition region, where a constant minimum roational speed is kept, the method
of pole placement is utilized. The simpliﬁed model used in this project assumes rigid turbine,
i.e. the drive train is a rigid body, quasi-static aerodynamics and no ﬁlters or pitch actuators are
included [29]. Under these assumptions the open-loop non-linear equation of the drive train motion
can be written as:

Full Load Operation

2.5.2
TLP Model Control
Two different controllers were tested for the scaled ﬂoating wind turbine and were designed by
Researcher M. Mirzaei. The ﬁrst controller was an onshore controller and the second one was
a ﬂoating wind turbine controller. This section details both control systems and the difference
between them.
Wind turbine controllers are normally tuned to follow the described mentioned in section 2.5.1. In
the case of the scaled DTU 10MW wind turbine, the main objective of the tuning is to match the
scaled thrust curve so that the dynamics of the scaled ﬂoating wind turbine resembles the behaviour
of the full scale turbine. Therefore normal tuning procedures were not used and the generator
torque controller for the partial load region was tuned by trial and error, so that the thrust force
matched the theoretical one as close as possible.
An important aspect of control design is to ensure that the excitation frequencies do not coincide
with the natural frequencies of the structure. Moreover wind turbines are also effected by the 1P and
3P frequencies from the rotating blades, but also the wave frequency, which have to be taken into
account in the design phase. If however the excitation forces coincide with the natural frequencies
of the structure it can result in severe oscillations (resonance) and if not damped the structure might
be damaged or lead to fatigue over time. With an operational range of Ω= [53.02; 74.37] rpm,
a wave frequency climate range of fwave = [0.631.19] Hz and natural frequencies determined in
section 5.4.4, the excitation frequencies can be sketched as shown in Figure 2.9.
25
==PAGE:46==
Control System
Figure 2.9: Excitation frequencies from the six degrees of freedom, rotational speed of the rotor
and the waves.
Onshore Controller
The onshore controller, is tuned such that control actions do not excite the dynamics of the structure.
One method of designing such controller is to use pole-placement method and place poles of the
closed-loop system below that of the lowest frequency of the structure of the wind turbine. For
more details on the method of pole-placement for tuning wind turbine controller see [30].
Floating Wind Turbine Controller
For the ﬂoating wind turbine, the control task is more challenging than the onshore wind turbine.
As mentioned in the previous section, the controller should be designed such that the frequency of
the structure and platform are not excited by control action, which means poles of the closed loop
control system should be chosen below the lowest structure/platform frequency.
However, for the ﬂoating wind turbine, the frequency of the platform motion was very low and
therefore the tuning of the controller was adjusted in order to avoid instability. This was only
needed for the full load region, and the partial load region controller for the ﬂoating wind turbine
was the same as the onshore controller.
26
==PAGE:47==
CHAPTER 3
Experimental Setup
This test campaign was carried out at DHI (Danish Hydraulic Institute) situated in Hørsholm in
Denmark. Is has a large 30 m × 22 m basin with a depth of 3 m, which is shown in Figure 3.1 seen
from above as a schematic drawing. 60 individual wave paddles a⃝are installed on the front side
of the basin which enables creation of unidirectional waves, misaligned waves and spread waves
(3D). In the opposite end of the wave paddles a "beach" - parabolic wave absorber c⃝is placed
which minimizes the reﬂection of the waves. Foam is placed along the sides of the basin, in order
to reduce the transversal reﬂection of the waves.
A large wind generator b⃝with an outlet area of 4m × 4m was placed on the front side above
the wave makers, such that the ﬂoating wind turbine could be tested in a climate consisting of
both wind and waves. The ﬂoater g⃝was installed in the water, such that the distance between
the nacelle and the front of the wind generator was approximately 4 m as sketched in Figure 3.2.
Multiple sensors were installed on the wind turbine and all the signals were captured by a data
acquisition device d⃝, which was situated on the control bridge f⃝, where from the experiments
were controlled and monitored. Wave gauges (WG) were placed in the water to measure the wave
elevation, as seen in Figure 3.1, at eleven different places in the basin. However, only nine were
used during the experiments where the ﬂoater was in water, since WG 11 and WG 7 had coordinates
coinciding with the position of the ﬂoater. These two wave gauges were therefore only used in
preliminary tests to validate the wave climate. Part of the sensors were the motion tracking system,
Qualisys, which consisted of two cameras and some tracking markers, which will be described in
more detail later. However, the approximate location of the cameras e⃝are seen in Figure 3.1.
This section will present the chosen environmental conditions, in which the ﬂoating wind turbine
was tested in, the sensors, the acquisition system and details about the general setup.
27
==PAGE:48==
CHAPTER 3. EXPERIMENTAL SETUP

measure on the drawing. It is only for illustrative purpose.

conditions. The environmental conditions consist of combinations of signiﬁcant wave height Hs,
wave peak period Tp and mean wind speed V10 and a large amount of combinations of the three
parameters exists. Surely not all environmental conditions could be tested, which was why four
was chosen for the test campaign, as seen in Table 3.1.

According to [25] the DTU 10MW RWT has a rated wind speed of 11.4 m/s, which means that
rated wind speed of the model scale version in focus is approximately 1.47 m/s. From Table 3.1, it
is therefore seen that sea state 3 is below rated, 5 is just about rated and 6 and 8 are above rated.
Importantly, it should by noted that the wind generators, used to generate the wind ﬁeld, had a
maximum rotational speed of 1800 RPM, which was estimated to correspond to a maximum wind
speed of approximately 2.2 m/s, see Section 4.1.1. It was therefore decided to use this maximum
wind speed at sea state 6 and 8, although sea state 8 has a wind speed above cut-out. Furthermore,
it should be noted that the wave-characteristics of the sea state above 8 were close to the limit of
what the wave-paddles could generate which is why EC 8 was the highest.
It should be pointed out, that [9] constructed the waves with a 5% steepness, which is calculated by
the usage of the deep water wave length, L0, or the peak period as:

3.2
Wind Generators
The wind generators were designed by the authors of [9] as part of the previous test campaign. A
full description of the design and the design process can be found in that report, hence only a brief
description of the wind generator will be presented in the following.
Since the crane at DHI has a lifting limit of one tonne and a single motor with fan weigh 100 kg, it
was decided to design 6 individual and identical wind generators with the outlet dimensions of 2 m
× 1.33 m, in order to avoid weight problems and make it more convenient to move around. The
reason for these dimensions is that the rotor diameter is 2.972 m, which means that when the 6
units are placed together, they will cover an area of 4 × 4 meters, which is covering the rotor area
sufﬁciently. The wind generators can be seen in Figure 3.4(a).
Each wind generator consists of a motor, an AVL-710 fan, four screens and ten guide vanes with
the purpose of turning the ﬂow 90◦, without signiﬁcant energy loss. The guide vanes also reduce
the large scale turbulence caused by the fans. This solution also excluded the need for a diffuser,
which made the design more compact. According to [9] these screens did not cause signiﬁcant
amount of pressure losses. An exploded view of a single unit can be seen in Figure 3.4(b).
Due to the limited wind speed in the ﬁrst test campaign of 2015, it was decided to change the
motors before the second campaign, which was done primarily by the authors of [16]. However,
the providers could not deliver in time, which eventually meant that ﬁve motors of type LAFERT
HPS90 1800 84 were installed, together with one LAFERT HPS90 3600 84. The latter motor had
signiﬁcantly different torque characteristics, which meant that it was running more than 100%
when blowing the max 2.1 m/s at 1800 RPM. However, since the fans were identical, it performed
as good as the ﬁve other generators, which made it possible to get a good steady wind ﬁeld. The
30
==PAGE:51==
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.4: Left: The six units placed together.[9]. Right: Exploded view of a single wind
generator unit. [9]
motors were manually controlled by six ABB 4 kW 400V frequency converters.
It was mentioned earlier that the area of the outlet was big enough to cover the rotor-area. However,
since the wave paddles had a certain height above the MWL, the generators had to be positioned
640 mm above SWL, resulting in the generators not being able to cover the entire rotor area as
sketched in Figure 3.2 and which can also be seen in the contour plots of Figure 4.3.
3.3
Mooring Line System
The mooring system consists of three tensioned lines, each one tied to a leg of the platform and to
an anchor point situated vertically below the fairlead on the seabed. A wooden frame was mounted
on the sea bed in order to lead the mooring lines from the anchor point using small metal eyes and
up to the control bridge where they were fastened. The lines were looped through the metal eyes in
such a way that no relative ’slip’ between the line and the eye could occur. This enabled setting the
correct tensions and draft of the platform, but also ensuring the correct elastic line length of the
mooring lines. The mooring lines used in test campaign were Dyneema® ﬁber rope with a diameter
of 2.2 mm. A schematic of the mooring system setup is shown in Figure 3.5. The target platform
draft was achieved at the beginning of each test day, by ensuring the design mean sea level line on
the model was at the free surface.
31
==PAGE:52==
Measurement Equipment
Figure 3.5: Lab scale mooring system setup
3.4
Measurement Equipment
As mentioned earlier, many different sensors were involved in this test campaign to capture
information. The amount of sensors varied between different kind of tests, such as measuring the
wind ﬁeld, calibrating the waves, Rotor ID tests of the clamped wind turbine and the actual tests
with the turbine mounted on the ﬂoater. This section will present the measurement equipments used
throughout the test campaign and how they were calibrated, where the list is shown in Table 3.2.

Originally it was wanted to measure the x, y, z accelerations of the ﬂoater as well, but since the
32
==PAGE:53==
installation was complicated and time was short, it was decided to disregards those, and only use
the motion recorded by Qualisys for the response of the ﬂoater.
All the signals from the ’equipment’ column of Table 3.2 were analogue signals that were being
fed into a data acquisition system belonging to DHI and were logged in the DHI computer with a
frequency of 160 Hz. The signals coming from the two JVL MAC050 46W Integrated Servo Motors,
generator and pitch motor, located at the hub were digital signals and were logged/controlled by a
computer that DTU brought to the site. In order to log all signals into one single ﬁle, DTU also
brought two USB-6218 DAQ units from National Instruments. The analogue signals from the DHI
system was then fed into the DTU USB-6218 DAQ units, which was logging with 50 Hz. Each test
was logged on both the DTU computer and the DHI computer, although the DHI ﬁles would not
have the motor data. Both systems could handle an input voltage of ± 10 volts. A schematic of the
hardware setup can be seen in Figure 3.6.
Figure 3.6: Schematic of the hardware setup [17].
The DHI system consisted of three standard cabinets, two ﬁlter cabinets and one transmitting cabinet.
Each standard cabinet had eight slots for interchangeable ampliﬁers, since an accelerometer and a
strain gauge for instance requires different ampliﬁers. From here, the signals were fed into the ﬁlter
cabinets after which they were transmitted to the computer where they were logged by the DHI
logging software ’Wave Synthesizer’, which was also used to control the waves.
It should also be noted that the gain and the zero could be adjusted on each individual ampliﬁer.
When these were set during calibration, they were locked to ensure they remained constant though
out the test campaign.
Wave Gauges
As mentioned earlier, nine or eleven wave gauges were used depending on the tests. But they
were calibrated in the same way. Contrary to usual practice, a new way of calibrating the wave
33
==PAGE:54==
Measurement Equipment
gauges was implemented. Traditionally each wave gauge is taken out of the water and calibrated
in a bucket of water where the gauge is moved exactly 10 cm up and down. In this way the gain
was set on each wave gauge. In order to save time, a method where the wave paddles were moved
from rear to front position causing an increase in water level of 3.5 cm was tested. Experts from
DHI estimated that 6 cm difference in water level would result in a proper calibration. The new
method of calibrating the wave gauges was assessed by testing if the wave gauges could measure
the exact 10 cm difference of water level in the bucket. The maximum deviation from the 10 cm
reference was 1 mm. Thus, it was accepted that all wave gauges could be calibrated simultaneously
by the new method. The wave gauges were cleaned and calibrated each morning before running
production tests.
Z-gauges and Floater Positioning
As mentioned earlier, the ﬂoater was a TLP with three spokes, thus three mooring lines were
mounted and numbered as in Figure 3.7, where it is seen that spoke number 3 was pointed upwind.
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.7: Left: Seen from the control bridge above the water surface. The wooden frame base
and the ﬂoater are shown. Right: Sketch of the the mooring line labeling.
The tension in the mooring lines was measured by a Z-gauge on each line. The Z-gauges functioned
as a link 50 – 100 cm above the bottom of the basin. The position close to the bottom was chosen
in order to avoid them moving too much with the ﬂoater, which would make them function as an
extra mass to the ﬂoater. The calibration was carried out onshore by suspending the Z-gauges to a
10 kg weight. Hence a relationship between the loading and the voltage output was obtained. By
adjusting the gain, the voltage output could be read as force given in Newtons.
Unfortunately the z-gauge mounted on mooring line number 2 short-circuited due to water intake.
34
==PAGE:55==
The other two Z-gauges kept tight, however their calibration point was seen to be ﬂoating. Z-gauge
1 measured 90.3 N and Z-gauge 3 measured 96.3 N when exposed to a 10 kg weight after the test
campaign.
Strain Gauges
The fore-aft and side-side shear force in the tower top, representing the thrust and the side force
for the tests with wind, could be measured by a two component gauge mounted as a link between
the nacelle and the tower top. The two component gauge consists of four beams with two strain
gauges attached to each and then connected to a full Wheatstone bridge circuit. Since the gauge is
two component two bridges are needed and the conﬁguration of each bridge allows only a voltage
output when the beams are exposed to either a lateral or a transverse force.
The two component gauge was calibrated by exposing the fore-aft and the side-side direction with
known weights of 1-5 kg, which would allow the following linear relation to be applied between
force and output voltage:
T[N] = V [V] × 5 N
1 V
(3.2)
A piece of paper was wrapped around the the strain gauge with no direct contact in order to reduce
the interference of wind on the circuits, as the wind can cool down the aluminium beams. This can
be seen in Figure 3.8(b).
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.8: Left: Picture of the strain gauges glued onto the four beam link between tower and
nacelle. Right: Visualization of the paper wrapped around the link between nacelle and tower.
Accelerometers
Two accelerometers were fastened to the nacelle right in front of the strain gauge, as seen in Figure
3.8(b), perpendicular to each other enabling the measurement of fore-aft and side-side accelerations.
35
==PAGE:56==
Measurement Equipment
The accelerometers were calibrated one at a time by placing them on a levelled horizontal plate,
which would give a certain voltage level corresponding to the acceleration of gravity. Since the
accelerometers could measure ± 2 times the gravitational acceleration and since the input voltage
to the logging system was limited to ± 10 V, it was decided to set the gain such that:
2g = 10V −→acc [m/s2] = V [V] · 1.964[m/s2/V]
(3.3)
The static gravitational calibration was done for both directions.
Qualisys
The rigid-body motion of the ﬂoating wind turbine in all six degrees of freedom was captured
with a motion tracking system, Qualisys. A set of tracking markers was placed on the structure -
two on the lower part of the tower and three additional markers mounted on an aluminium frame
fastened to the transition between tower and ﬂoater as seen in Figure 3.9. The position of all ﬁve
markers were tracked by two infrared cameras with 100 Hz sampling rate and transformed into
real-time analogue voltage signals of the surge, sway, heave, roll, pitch and yaw motion. The
original digital signal was also transmitted to a Qualisys computer on which the data could be
seen live in a 3D space. The location of the two cameras can be seen in Figure 3.1. Before the
experiments, the orientation, focus and aperture of the infrared cameras were adjusted to ensure
the highest possible quality of measured motion within the measurement volume. Furthermore,
calibration of the Qualisys system was carried out so the cameras knew their position with respect
to the origin of coordinates, which was placed at the centre of the ﬂoater at still water level. This
calibration was done by following the Qualisys manual belonging to DHI. It should be noted that
the accuracy of the translation motion was within 1 mm, which with the applied spacing of the
tracking objects corresponds to an accuracy in the order of 0.1◦for rotary motion.
Figure 3.9: The position of the ﬁve motion tracker markers.
Wind Probes
Air Velocity Transducers (TSI 8455) suitable for low wind speeds, have been used for measuring
the wind ﬁeld [31]. The Air Velocity Transducer (AVT) used provides a linear electric signal, in
36
==PAGE:57==
this case voltage in the range of 0 −10 V, dependent on the wind speed. It consists of a probe with
a rugged ceramic sensor in a housing that only allows unidirectional 2D ﬂow to pass the sensor.
During the tests, one AVT was placed a meter from the two lowest wind generators. The certainty
of the measurements is ±2 % or ±0.5 % of full scale of selected range. The repeatability is within
±1.0 % of reading and the AVT’s are calibrated once a year externally, since this requires special
facilities.
Wave Paddle
The signal from a paddle located in the centre of the ﬂume (no. 31) was recorded in order to allow
the reuse of the signal for numerical reproduction in OceanWave3D [32]. In order to know the
range of the paddle strokes, the voltage input to the wave paddle was measured as it was moved
from rear, to middle position and to the most forward position. The signal was in the range -5 to +5
V. In addition, the stroke length of the piston was measured. If the middle position is taken as zero,
the distances to the rear position is -31 cm while the distance to the front position is 28 cm.
37
==PAGE:58==
==PAGE:59==
CHAPTER 4
Measurement Campaign
This section presents the essence of the experimental study. First, the environmental conditions
at the test facilities will be analysed and validated against the target climate. Then, the results
from the free decay tests of the ﬂoating wind turbine will be analysed and the structural natural
frequencies and damping ratios will be determined. Lastly, the results from the main tests are
presented, consisting of the response to regular, irregular and focused waves. In each of these result
sections the general response of the structure will be investigated without wind and compared to
case with wind. Additionally, a controller comparison in each wave type is also carried out. In
the case of irregular waves the effect of misaligned waves and also spread waves will be analysed,
whereas in the focused wave case the effect of controller shutdown will be presented. The last
section will focus on the results with inverted waves and how to extract the linear and higher order
response based on a state of the art method.
4.1
Climate Validation
In this section it will be investigated to what extent the environmental conditions resemble the
target climates, recall Section 3.1. Firstly, the wind climate will be analysed, followed by the wave
climate and lastly the results from the bottom ﬁxed rotor performance tests will presented.
4.1.1
Wind Fields
The wind climate was generated by six individual wind generators, as described in section 3.2 and
before the turbine was mounted the wind climate was analysed. In order to obtain the right wind
speed for the down-scaled model experiments the wind ﬁeld was measured and the required fan rpm
for the motors was noted. The wind speeds were measured in the plane at the wind turbine position,
i.e. 4 m from the wind generators, using four Air velocity Transducers. A ﬁfth and ﬁnal wind probe
was mounted right in front of the lower right wind generator to be logged in the experiments. A
wooden frame with a steel rail on top was constructed and placed in the transversal direction at
the position of the turbine. This would allow a cart to sweep across the wind ﬁeld at the turbine
position operated with a robe from the main bridge. By then mounting a mast with 12 different
levels on the cart the four of the TSI wind probes could be attached to it, with maximum distance
39
==PAGE:60==
Climate Validation
between each probe. This would mean that three sweeps were completed where the ﬁrst being with
probe positions [1, 4, 7, 10], second with [2, 5, 8, 11] and third with [3, 6, 9, 12]. Furthermore, is
was decided to sweep using ﬁve positions along the rail, i.e. [0, ±0.8, ±1.6] m from the turbine
position and measure for 2 min every position corresponding to 15.5 min full scale time.
The corresponding heights of the wind probes above MWL were ranging from 1 m to 4.61 m above
mean water level and the full setup of the wind measuring unit can be seen in Figure 4.1.
Figure 4.1: Wind ﬁeld measuring unit consisting of a mast with attached wind probes mounted on
a cart.
The fan rpm range for the motors and the corresponding wind speeds measured at approximately
hub height (1.95 m above MWL) around the center on the rail, i.e. turbine position, can be seen in
Figure 4.2. The maximum velocity of the fans was 1800 rpm, resulting in a mean wind speed V of
2.1-2.3 m/s, which means that the environmental states (shown in Table 3.1) above 6 could not be
reached with the correctly scaled wind speed, hence the maximum wind speed was applied to these
climates (see Table 4.1).
From Figure 4.2 it can be seen that the mean wind speed depends linearly on the fan velocity, hence
a linear correlation between them has been determined in order to determine the right wind speed
for the down-scaled model experiments:
V ≈−0.1280 + 0.0013 · Vfan,rpm
(4.1)
Where V is the mean velocity [m/s] and Vfan,rpm is the motor velocity [rpm].

TI = σ

(4.2)
The mean velocity and turbulence intensity ﬁelds are presented as contour plots in Figure 4.3
for EC3, EC5 and EC6 & 8. The rotor position is sketched by a black circle and as it is evident
the measurements do not cover the entire rotor area as described in the end of section 3.2. This
means that in the azimuthal range 135 −225 deg the outer region of the blades will experience a
signiﬁcant drop in wind speed and signiﬁcantly increased turbulence intensity. The area outside the
measurement area was estimated to be approximately 11% of the total rotor area, which will cause
a load variation of the rotor.
Furthermore, the ﬁgure shows that for the majority of the rotor area, the wind ﬁeld is relatively
uniform and has low turbulence with an overall mean turbulence intensity across wind speeds of
about 3.5%. This means that the performance of the wind generators is overall satisfying.
Lastly, it can be observed that the wind ﬁeld in the lower left corner of the swept area is exposed to
high turbulence and a signiﬁcant low wind speed. This can be due to construction mistake of the
lower left wind generator, as stated in [9], where the guiding vanes have been installed with a too
large angle, resulting in the ﬂow being directed to the center of the domain instead of making the
ﬂow uniform.

4.1.2
Wave Climates
Despite the control ﬁles for the wave maker at DHI were reused from a previous test campaign [9]
carried out in 2015, the wave ﬁelds were quality checked to ensure they had the correct behaviour.
Before the ﬂoater were placed in the basin three tests were carried out for the most severe sea state,
EC8, namely regular, irregular and a focused wave. However, only the validation of the focused
wave will be shown from these initial test. For the regular and irregular cases, all four sea states
were validated based on wave gauge 8 and 9 while the ﬂoater was in the basin. The measured
wave elevation and period has been compared to the target values computed with linear wave theory.
The comparison for regular waves is shown in Fig. 4.4 where all four sea states are presented in a
10 s interval. A deviation between WG8 and WG9 is seen for EC3 and is included here, to show
the importance of validating the measurement from one wave gauge with other wave gauges. This
has been done throughout this project to avoid errors. In EC8 non-linear effects, i.e. a small set-up,
developed in the basin and is clearly seen when comparing with the target wave elevation which do
not take these effects into account. The wave periods show a good match, as presented in Table 4.2.
The wave height of the regular waves show a decent match, and was accepted for the test campaign.
The irregular waves are validated based on their corresponding wave spectra. Figure 4.5 shows

4.1.3
Rotor Thrust
As it is described in section 2.4.1 in the downscaling of offshore platforms, priority is traditionally
given to the preservation of the Froude number Fr. This means that the Re number is not preserved,
which represents an issue when aerodynamics are involved — e.g. downscaling of ﬂoating wind
turbines. In our case, this means that for a given wind speed V the downscaled rotor will generally
produce a different mean thrust force T than the reference DTU 10MW wind turbine for the same
operational conditions — namely rotor speed and blade pitch angle. The aerodynamic thrust is the
most important aerodynamic element in the overall dynamics of a ﬂoating wind turbine, therefore
it is desired to match the scaled thrust of the reference wind turbine for each wind speed. In the
experimental setup, the rotor speed was always set to the downscaled reference curve, and the
match of the reference steady thrust curve was carried out by modifying the blade pitch settings for
each wind speed until the mean thrust was equal to the target (see Fig. 4.7).

In addition, it was decided to obtain the sensitivity of the thrust force to the blade pitch. Therefore,
once the blade pitch θ that provided the desired thrust was found, the thrust was also measured for
blade pitch values corresponding to θ −2, θ −1, θ + 1 and θ + 2 deg. In Fig. 4.7 (bottom, red
45
==PAGE:66==
Initial Structural Response
line) we show the pitch settings necessary in the experimental turbine to obtain the reference thrust
(top, blue line). The thrust curves for the sensitivity analysis correspond to deviations of ± 1 and 2
deg from θ (top, black and green lines: +1 and +2 deg and bottom, black and green lines: -1 and -2
deg). However, for wind speed 1.7 m/s, the case where the blade pitch is θ −2 produced too much
aerodynamic torque and the wind turbine motor could not handle it. Therefore, for this wind speed
the sensitivity analysis was carried out for pitch angles θ −2, θ −1, θ + 1 and θ + 2 deg. For the
same reason, the sensitivity analysis for wind speeds 2.0 and 2.2 m/s was carried out with blade
pitch settings θ −1, θ + 1 and θ + 2 deg.
4.2
Initial Structural Response
The response of the ﬂoating wind turbine can now be examined in different environmental states
and with different operational conditions using the data output from the Qualisys (6 DOFs), the
two accelerometers and the tendon gauges. First, the wave only tests were conducted with idle
turbine followed by the wave and wind cases with both control 1 (offshore controller described in
section 2.3.4) and a land-based type controller (described in section 2.3.3). Lastly, some shut down
scenarios were conducted in the full load region, i.e. EC 6 and EC 8.
4.2.1
Post-Processing
Several ﬁlters were applied to the raw data from the measurement equipments in order to remove
noise, spikes and non-physically high frequency components. First a Butterworth lowpass ﬁlter
was used on all signal outputs with a normalized cutoff frequency of 0.25 corresponding to 25%
of the sample rate. The order of the lowpass ﬁlter was set to 4, but higher orders could have been
involved as it would have yielded better off-frequency rejection, but at the expense of a longer
impulse response and a little more computation expense. The Butterworth lowpass ﬁlter is used
to ﬁlter off all unnecessary high frequency components which are out of the scope of the model
measurements.
Next, a Median ﬁlter was applied to all Qualisys measurements, as rapid spikes were observed on
most of the signals at severe sea states, i.e. EC6 and EC8. These spikes were mostly due to water
splashing up the turbine, hence making the Qualisys markers wet and thereby disturbing the signal.
The median ﬁlter replaces every segment of points speciﬁed by the user in the signal data by the
median of each segment. For 15 points were used to specify each segment. However using this
kind of ﬁlter some of the higher frequency components, which are within the scope of interest, can
be removed. The inﬂuence of the different ﬁlters can be seen 4.8.
The spikes from the water on the sensors were sometimes of longer duration meaning that the low
number of points in median ﬁlter, which should be as low as possible, were not enough. Instead of
increasing the bandwidth of the Median ﬁlter, another custom ﬁlter was constructed in Matlab to
simply cut off the long spikes from the data based on a 3σ-threshold. The roll signal seemed to be
badly hit by these spikes of long duration when testing irregular waves, as shown in Figure 4.8,
46
==PAGE:67==
meaning that the median ﬁlter of 15 points would capture some of the peaks and not ﬁlter them
away.

4.2.2
Free Decay Tests
The free decay tests of the rigid body motion of the ﬂoating wind turbine were carried out by
imposing an initial displacement to the structure in order to understand the system characteristics
such as natural frequencies and the system damping. This was done by attaching a length of line
on different points on the structure. In surge and sway, the line was attached to the ﬂoater at the
waterline level. In roll and pitch, the line was attached some distance along the wind turbine tower.
In yaw, the line was attached to the fairlead on the front spoke. Due to the relatively high tensions
and stiffness of the system in heave, it was not possible to carry out a satisfactory free decay test
in heave.Still, a tentative estimate is listed in Table 4.4. For each degree of freedom, several free
decays were done in succession. Initial displacements of varying amplitudes were imposed in an
attempt to capture varying levels of linear and nonlinear behaviour.
Table 4.4 shows the identiﬁed natural frequencies and damping ratios for the six degrees of freedom.
Due to the limitations of the method in applying the initial displacement for each degree of freedom,
it was not possible to completely isolate an initial displacement in the desired degree of freedom
without inducing an initial displacement in other degrees of freedom. This is especially seen for
surge and pitch, and sway and roll due to the strong couplings between these degrees of freedom.

4.2.3
Response Amplitude Operators
This section describes the obtained Response Amplitude Operators (RAO) for surge, heave and
pitch motion. The RAO’s reveal valuable information about the response of the structure exposed
to waves of different frequencies. In principal, the RAO is a transfer function based on linear wave
theory and a linear relation between forcing and response, meaning the structure responds at the
same frequency as the incoming waves. The RAO for each direction of motion is deﬁned as [33],
RAO(f) =
s
Sξi(f)
Sη(f)
,
i = 1, . . . , 6,
(4.3)
where Sξi denotes the energy spectrum of the response and Sη the wave energy spectrum.
The wave spectrum used to determine the RAO’s is based on a white noise signal. Hence, waves at
all frequencies contain the same amount of energy. The white noise signal used for this analysis
generated waves in the frequency range from 0.15 Hz to 2.5 Hz. Outside of this range the wave
energy decreases signiﬁcantly. Thus, the RAO curves are computed only in the stated frequency
range due to possible non-reliable results outside of the range.
A median ﬁlter, moving average of 15 points, has been applied to the response and wave energy
spectrum. This has been done in order to cope with single high peaks due to noise. Figure 4.9
shows the RAO’s for surge, heave and pitch motion obtained experimentally. The graph is shown
in the interval [0.15; 2.5] Hz. Sway, roll and yaw is not presented here since the waves used to
determine the RAO’s were aligned with the structure. Thus, great uncertainty may be present for
the RAO of sway, roll and yaw.
From Figure 4.9 it is noticeable that all three RAO’s presented is dominated by surge motion. On
the contrary, the pitch natural frequency is not visible in the RAO. This implies that the designed
TLP is very stiff in this degree of freedom. Regular waves are used as an comparison and are
represented by a red cross in the ﬁgure. It is seen that the regular wave cases conﬁrms the trend of
the RAO while not being close to the critical response frequency, i.e. surge natural frequency.

4.3
Results with Regular Waves
The regular waves are well-suited for identifying the general structure response, since their simplic-
ity allows a faster analysis due to their sinusoidal behaviour. However, they are not representative
of real sea states.
As described in Section 3.1 the experimental regular waves consists of four different Environmental
Conditions (EC), where each EC involved aligned waves, misaligned waves, without and with wind
for three different turbine control mechanisms; a controller tuned for offshore, a controller tuned
for onshore and an open loop controller with ﬁxed blade pitch and rotational speed according to
the mean wind speed at hub height. However, the analysis of regular waves in this section will
mainly focus on the effect of applying wind in the tests and then to compare the different control
systems of the turbine. This means that the misaligned regular wave cases will not be presented in
this analysis but will be presented in Section 5.6 to validate the numerical models. It should be
noted that every regular wave test had a duration of 240 seconds, but only 10 second windows out
of the full duration will be presented.

(also noted "ﬁxed") for each EC. The operational parameters for the wind turbine were determined
as described in Section 4.1.3.

4.3.1
Response to Regular Waves
The wave elevation measured by the two in-line wave gauges, WG8 and WG9, (recall Figure 3.1)
and form the basis of the dynamic response of the structure, can be seen in Figure 4.4.
First, the response to regular waves with and without wind is investigated for each environmental
condition. The wind cases in this analysis are based on the open loop controller ("ﬁxed") with the
above described operational parameters. Due to the regular waves being unidirectional and the
symmetry of the ﬂoater, no response in sway, roll and yaw motions was expected theoretically.
However, the experimental results show some response in these degrees of freedom, which can be
explained by the asymmetries introduced by imperfect ﬂoater alignment that can be very difﬁcult
to avoid in experimental setups. There will be some impacts on the motion in roll and yaw, when
wind is applied due to the rotor dynamics and turbulent wind, but in the following only the in-plane
motions (surge, heave and pitch) are shown, while the rest can be seen in Appendix D.1.
The platform motions are presented with the corresponding unﬁltered data. Figures 4.10−4.12
show the in-plane motions, surge, heave and pitch. It can be seen in the surge response, that the
effect of the applied Butterworth ﬁlter is not noticeable, since little noise is present. However,
when looking at the heave signal, which is signiﬁcantly smaller than the surge, the effect of the
Butterworth ﬁlter is much stronger, especially for the smaller sea states where the signal is within
the accuracy of the Qualisys system. Also, major spikes in the raw pitch response can be observed
for the higher sea states, which is due to the Qualisys markers mounted at the tower bottom getting
wet and thereby disturbing the signals. The effect of the median ﬁlter can be seen as it ﬁlters out
these unwanted spikes.
It is evident from the surge, heave and pitch that when applying wind to the structure, the aero-
dynamic thrust force causes a permanent displacement of the platform in the three degrees of
freedom, compared to the results without wind where the signals have a zero mean. The magnitude
of mean displacement increases from EC3 to EC5 as the thrust force increases to maximum at rated
conditions. In full load the blades are pitched, which means the thrust force is reduced, hence the

In Figure 4.15 the in-plane motion of the platform can be seen, for the other degrees of freedom see
Appendix D.2. In the surge at rated conditions (EC5) sub-harmonic nature is observed, oscillating
with the natural frequency of surge, 0.24 Hz. Furthermore, it can be seen that the onshore controller
results in higher surge response than the offshore- and open loop controller. At EC8, however, the
surge motions for all three controllers are relatively the same, since the forcing is governed by the
waves and the same tendencies can be observed in the heave motion.
When looking at pitch motion at rated conditions, a permanent displacement due to the wind can
be seen for all three controllers, but is higher for the onshore and ﬁxed controllers compared to the
offshore. However, the angle difference is small. Above rated the amplitude of the pitch oscillations
54
==PAGE:75==
are larger for the onshore controller than the two others, which is expected since the onshore
controller is much more "sensitive", meaning the blades are pithed more often. The onshore and
offshore controller achieves a higher pitch amplitude at EC8 due to the turbine moving downwind,
because of the impact of a wave crest. Then the controller "sees" too low torque, hence it reacts by
pitching more into the wind, i.e. lowering the blade pitch angle. When the wave trough approaches
the ﬂoating structure it will move in the upwind direction, hence with the current low pitch angle
set by the controller, the turbine will now "see" a too high power and it will react by pitching out of
the wind and so on.
In Figure 4.16 the nacelle fore-aft acceleration can be seen and the side-side is shown in Figure D.6.
At rated wind speed all three controllers achieve approximately the same nacelle accelerations, but
as the wind and waves increase the onshore controller gives rise to high frequency vibrations, as
seen in EC8.
