the onshore controller is constant at 1. It can be observed that the magnitude of the oscillations at
rated conditions for all three controllers are approximately the same but the mean tension in the
downwind mooring lines are lower for the onshore controller compared to the two others. This
is due to the larger permanent displacement in the pitch motion for the onshore controller. As
mentioned earlier, the waves are governing the dynamics at the highest environmental conditions,
which can also be seen in mooring line tension at EC8. The permanent displacement in pitch is
lower above rated, due to the decreased aerodynamic force, hence the mean tension for the front
mooring line is lower, i.e. closer to 1, and similarly for the downwind mooring lines. It can also be
observed that the effect of the "sensitive" blade pitch reaction of the onshore controller results in
larger oscillations in the front mooring line.

4.4
Results with Irregular Waves
In this section the response of the ﬂoating wind turbine will be investigated for all four irregular
sea states and the effect of wind, wave misalignment, control and spread waves will be analysed
respectively.
As described in Section B.2 the irregular waves consists of several wave groups with different
heights and lengths, hence they are best suited to represent real sea states. However due to the
random nature of irregular waves the comparison of responses in time series becomes impractical,
therefore the exceedance probability P is introduced. The exceedance probability plots simplify
the comparison of large amount of data:
P = p(X ≥xi) = 1 −
i
N + 1
(4.4)
For each signal and for each environmental state the peaks were identiﬁed and stored based on a
zero-crossing approach implemented as a MATLAB function by H. Bredmose, A. Pegalajar & M.
Borg (2016) for use in the LIFE50+ project and was kindly provided. The function takes the time
series of the incident wave and divide it into individual waves, where each wave is deﬁned between
two zero-crossings of the surface elevation. The maximum values of the speciﬁc signal are then
determined within each time range deﬁned by the individual waves and stored. The peaks are sorted
from minimum to maximum and assigned with an index i in order to compute the exceedance
probability of each peak based on its index and the total number of peaks N. This approach secures
the extreme events to be independent [14].
57
==PAGE:78==
Results with Irregular Waves
Figure 4.19 shows the distribution of independent wave amplitudes in the chosen irregular sea
states, see table 3.1, measured by WG8 and WG9. It can be seen that the wave gauges match. The
deviation between the two wave gauges can be due to transverse differences in the otherwise long-
crested waves. Also as the sea state increases the maximum wave amplitudes increase signiﬁcantly,
reaching a maximum wave height of ≈0.32 m in seastate EC8.

It should be noted that in order to clearly identify the effect of wind and wave misalignment in the
following, the six degrees of freedom motions were subtracted their mean, in order to remove the
static offset component seen for the regular waves.
4.4.1
Response to Irregular Waves
The corresponding six degrees of freedom platform motion to the irregular waves presented in
Figure 4.19 is presented in Figure 4.20 with wind (x) and without wind (o). It can be seen that the
surge response is generally only slightly damped when wind is applied, due to the aerodynamic
damping, with only EC8 showing signiﬁcantly higher damping of the response for P < 10%. The
sway motion on the other hand seem to be fairly independent of the wind. A minor difference
would be expected due to the rotating blades and the turbulent wind. It is seen that the sway
response is approximately an order smaller than the surge motion. As for the surge motion there is
a clear tendency of larger response for increasing sea state, but the sway probability curves are not
following the same pattern as the surge motion. This is especially noticeable for the smallest sea
state, EC3, and this behaviour can be due to the imperfect alignment of the model, the mooring
setup or the incoming waves.
58
==PAGE:79==
The heave response increases when wind is applied, which is be due to the permanent offset of the
turbine caused by the aerodynamic force forcing the structure slightly deeper into the water, due
to the coupling between surge and heave. This means that there will be a contribution from the
restoring force to the heave motion when displaced. However, it should be noted that the difference
in heave for the all the sea states is limited, which is a fundamental feature of a TLP.
Having an imperfect alignment of the model in the basin will also cause a rolling response of the
system and as expected it follows the same tendencies as sway. The only difference is the roll
motion at EC 6 where a large response without wind is observed, but no solid explanation for this
can be given.
The magnitude of the pitch response is approximately the same for all sea states for the cases
with and without wind, except at rated conditions where the aerodynamic damping effect can be
seen. This will be investigated further in the next section. When including wave misalignment the
increase in yaw motion is likely due to the turbulence of the wind ﬁeld or rotor imbalances. In
addition the undesired misalignment of the model, that causes the system to slightly roll, can result
in the aerodynamic force giving rise to a moment about the z-axis, thus increased yaw motion.
Figure 4.21 shows the nacelle accelerations and here the damping effect from the wind can be
observed in the fore-aft acceleration. In addition the fore-aft acceleration increases as the waves
grow in size. This tendency can be observed in the side-side acceleration too and when wind is
applied a larger response is achieved compared to the case without, which is due to the effect of the
rotor dynamics.
The normalised mooring line tensions are presented in Figure 4.22. The measured tensions increase
as the waves grow in size, due to the greater surge and pitch motion. When wind is applied, higher
loads can be observed in the front mooring line (τ3) but will also impose an offset in the pretension
due to the displacement of the structure caused by the wind. The front mooring line experiences
the largest increase in mean tension of ≈15% higher than the pretension at rated conditions. On
the other hand the downwind mooring lines experiences a decrease of ≈7% at EC5 compared to
the no wind case.
59

Results with Irregular Waves
4.4.2
Effect of Wave Misalignment
All irregular wave tests were conducted with a wave heading of β = 30◦as deﬁned in Figure 4.23.
The six degrees of freedom response of the ﬂoater platform was captured by the Qualisys system in
the global ﬂoater coordinate system. However, when dealing with misaligned waves, the response
is typically projected into the wave coordinate system, see Figure 4.23. This allows for a more
appropriate comparison of the effect of misalignment on the different degrees of freedom, hence
the surge, sway, roll, and pitch (ξ1,ξ2,ξ4,ξ5) in cases with misalignment were projected as described
in (4.5), while heave and yaw (ξ3 and ξ6) remained unchanged. It should also be noted that the
nacelle accelerations were kept in the ﬂoater coordinate system as a reference.
Figure 4.23: Wave coordinate system and global ﬂoater coordinate system.
Since the impact from the aerodynamic force is largest at rated conditions (EC5), this sea state is
again chosen for misalignment of wind and waves. As done in the previous section the response in
the six degrees of freedom for wind and without wind tests had their corresponding mean subtracted
from the signals to highlight the differences in the dynamics.
ˆξ1 = ξ1 cos(θ) + ξ2 sin(θ)
ˆξ4 = ξ4 cos(θ) + ξ5 sin(θ)
ˆξ2 = ξ2 cos(θ) −ξ1 sin(θ)
ˆξ5 = ξ5 cos(θ) −ξ4 sin(θ)
(4.5)
ˆξ3 = ξ3
ˆξ6 = ξ6
The effect of misalignment and wind on the six degrees of freedom for EC5 is shown in Figure 4.24.
The surge motion in aligned waves and without wind shows as expected the largest response and
the effect of the aerodynamic damping can be seen as the response with wind included decrease
faster. However, the damping effect is minor for the misaligned waves case, as only a component
of the wind will affect the surge. As expected, the magnitude of sway motions are all similar, since
the misaligned cases are projected into the wave coordinate system. If the signals did not have
62
==PAGE:83==
their mean value subtracted, a permanent offset for the wind and wave misaligned case would be
expected, since the wind would induce a component in the negative ˆy-direction.

Considering heave motion it is evident that the wind causes larger response due to the permanent
63
==PAGE:84==
Results with Irregular Waves
displacement, whereas it seems independent of the wave heading angle. For the roll motion a
damping effect can be observed for the misaligned case when wind is applied, which is due to the
wind component in the wave coordinate system for the misaligned cases. As mentioned earlier, the
impact from the aerodynamic force is largest at rated wind speed which can clearly be seen in the
pitch response where both wind cases are much more damped when compared to the no-wind cases.
It can be seen that the pitch reduces with ≈30 −40% when wind is included, with higher damping
for the aligned cases. The damping effect of wind is expected to act primarily in the direction of
the wind. Since yaw motion is independent of the coordinate transformation, it achieves the highest
response when the structure is exposed to misaligned waves.
The nacelle accelerations are not transformed, hence Figure 4.25 shows the fore-aft and side-side
accelerations in the global ﬂoater coordinate system. As expected, the highest fore-aft acceleration
are achieved in aligned waves, where the amplitudes are signiﬁcantly larger without the inclusion if
wind. The same picture is seen for side-side accelerations but with the highest response for the
misaligned cases.

4.4.3
Controller Comparison in Irregular Waves
In this section the performance of the three different control systems in aligned irregular waves will
be analysed as in Section 4.3.2. The ﬁgure labelsonshore and offshore refer to the controllers tuned
for onshore and offshore respectively, whereas ﬁxed is the reference case with open loop control.
Since the partial load controller is the same for the onshore and offshore controller, as described in
Section 2.5.2, EC3 will not be presented in this analysis.
Comparison of the controllers will be carried out based on the response at rated conditions, EC5,
but similar comparison in the most severe environmental condition, EC8, can be found in Appendix
E.2. The scope of the analysis is not to investigate the effect of wind or wave misalignment, but
to compare their individual performance, hence the means of the signal are not subtracted as was
done in the previous sections.
Figure 4.27 shows the inﬂuence of the three controllers on the six degrees of freedom. In general,
the offshore controller performs at least as good as the other controllers considering the translational
motions and for roll, pitch and yaw motion signiﬁcantly better. A slight offset in roll can be observed
for the onshore and ﬁxed controller, which is not present at the highest environmental condition
(EC8), see Figure E.2 in Appendix E.2. Overall the performance of the onshore controller results
in larger rotations than the offshore one and for surge response it performs to the same level as the
offshore controller. The sway motion is similar for the three controllers and approximately an order
below the surge motion.

Results with Irregular Waves
Corresponding blade pitch θp and rotational speed Ωare presented in Figure 4.30. Note that for
ﬁxed control a speciﬁc blade pitch angle is kept constant at 5◦, hence a constant straight curve is
seen. The "aggressiveness" of the onshore controller can be seen easily, as it pitches the blades to a
higher degree than the offshore controller. For the P = 50% quantile the offshore controller is at a
pitch angle of 4.6◦, whereas the onshore is at 6.2◦. Overall the mean pitch angle of onshore- and
offshore controller are slightly lower than the ﬁxed pitch angle, 4.8◦and 4.5◦respectively. This
may be explained by the blade pitch angle for the ﬁxed controller not being exactly optimum, as
there will be an uncertainty in matching the thrust force for the ﬁxed controller, as described in
Section 4.1.3. The effect of the "aggressive" pitching of the blades for the onshore controller causes
likewise the rotational speed to achieve the highest values of all the three controllers. The reason
for the discontinuous signals is that the resolution is low at this EC resulting in a step response of
the rotational speed, where a higher resolution can be seen for EC8 in Figure E.5 in the Appendix.
Lastly it can be seen that mean rotational speed is different for the open loop controller and the two
active controllers. The ﬁxed operates at around the maximum rotational speed Ωmax = 72.1 rpm,
whereas the offshore and onshore controller operates around Ω= 74.1 rpm. This might be due to
an incorrect input parameter to the controllers.

4.4.4
Spread Irregular Waves
As previously mentioned, irregular waves are the most appropriate way to represent real sea states
and so far the analysis has been carried out with long-crested unidirectional waves (2D), but real
ocean waves are three-dimensional. This section will compare the response of the model in irregular
unidirectional (2D) and spread waves (3D). Spread waves are very complex but are of great interest,
since they can be used to identify eventual dynamics that might be missing, when conducting
experiments in unidirectional sea states.
68
==PAGE:89==
In this analysis, spread waves in all EC will be presented and compared with the corresponding
unidirectional waves. In the following ﬁgures, the unidirectional waves will be marked with (x) and
spread waves with (o). In order to see the cleanest effect of spread waves the test runs without wind
are presented. Ideally, the wave amplitudes of the unidirectional and spread waves are supposed to
match each other and as Figure 4.31 shows, this is the case for the highest sea states. For EC 3 and
EC 5 the spread waves become consistently smaller in magnitude after the 30%- and 60% quantile
respectively.
0
0.05
0.1
0.15
0.2
η [m]
10−3
10−2
10−1
100
Pexc [-]
EC3
EC5
EC6
EC8
Figure 4.31: Distribution of wave amplitudes in 2D and 3D irregular sea states EC 3,5,6,8.
Unidirectional waves (x) and spread waves (o).
In Figure 4.32 the six degrees of freedom response in irregular waves, both unidirectional and
spread, for all four sea states are shown. The surge response is higher for the unidirectional
waves, with a constant difference between the unidirectional and the spread waves for all sea
states. On the other hand, it can be observed that sway motion increases signiﬁcantly in spread
wave climates, which is due to wave force components in the y-direction. The heave response is
practically unaffected with the largest difference in the lowest sea state EC3, but is within the limits
of inaccuracy.
Since increased sway motion is introduced with spread waves, an increase in the roll response is to
be expected. The reason for the heavily increase in roll for EC8 subsequent to the 40% quantile is
the presence of spikes in the signal, due to wet Qualisys markers. Comparing the roll and sway
motion introduced in the 3D wave ﬁeld it can be seen that the increase in roll motion is relatively
smaller compared to the increase in sway. Spread waves appear to considerably lower the pitch
motion of the structure. The fore-aft motion in (surge and pitch) being smaller for the spread waves,
but larger in the side-side direction (sway and roll), agrees with the fact that as the signiﬁcant wave
height is supposed to be equal for the unidirectional and the spread waves, the hydrodynamic force
in the direction of the waves cannot possibly become as large for the spread waves. The yaw motion
is increased signiﬁcantly for the spread waves, which is due to the non-uniform wave loading on
the spokes, resulting in a moment about the z-axis.

==PAGE:92==
Results with Extreme Focused Waves
4.5
Results with Extreme Focused Waves
Extreme events can be modelled by the use of focused wave groups, which have similarities to
the irregular wave type, as they too are composed of a sum of individual waves over a range of
frequencies. However, the difference is that the focused waves have no phase difference between
the individual waves, resulting in a constructive merge at a single location x0 and time t0, namely
the focus point, see Figure 4.35. The surface elevation η(x, t) for a group of focused waves can be
described by:
η(x, t) =
N
X
i=1
ai cos(ωi(t −t0) −ki(x −x0))
(4.6)
where ai is the amplitude, ωi is the frequency and ki is the wave number of the individual waves.
The maximum wave height, Hmax, for the focused wave signals is computed from the signiﬁcant
wave height Hs of the corresponding irregular sea state; Hmax = 1.86Hs [9].
The analysis will present the impact of a single large wave group on the structure giving insight
of the extreme wave effects. The ﬁrst part of the analysis, the extreme response of the structure
will be investigated for sea state EC5 and EC8 with no wind applied. In the second part the three
controller performances are for EC5 and lastly shutdown cases of the turbine in focused waves will
be presented and the impact on the motion of the structure.
All measurements are presented in their time series to the left column and with their corresponding
power spectrum in the right column in order to analyse the contributions from wave frequency and
system natural frequencies.
Figure 4.35: Largest focused wave breaking at the turbine position.
72
==PAGE:93==
4.5.1
Response to EC5 Extreme Focused Wave
First, the response to the extreme focused wave for EC5 is investigated. Figure 4.36 presents the
wave elevation η and surge, heave and pitch motion. For EC5 the wave peak frequency lies at
fwave = 1/Tp = 1.06 Hz. The impulsive excitation force from the focused wave group causes
the largest surge response of 0.04m to take place after the largest peak in the surface elevation.
It can be observed that the impact of the focused wave group causes a short term offset in mean
displacement of the surge motion, which is a nonlinear effect and will be investigated and discussed
in Section 6.4. After the focused wave group has passed the structure, the structure shows free
surge motion with a decay ratio. This is why a much higher energy content at the surge natural
frequency 0.24 Hz is obtained compared to the energy at wave frequency, as seen in the power
spectrum.

Figure 4.36: Surge, heave and pitch motion in EC 5 focused waves.
Initial oscillations of the heave motion can be observed and is generally very contaminated by noise
73
==PAGE:94==
Results with Extreme Focused Waves
and this is due to the magnitude of the heave motion being within the inaccuracy of the Qualisys
system. This means that no solid conclusion of time series can be conducted, but looking at the
corresponding power spectral density function an energy content can be observed at the surge
frequency (0.24 Hz), but also at its second and third harmonics 0.5 Hz and 7.4 Hz respectively.
However note that the response is very low and can be seen as the ﬂoater being in equilibrium state.
The largest peak of the pitch motion (0.29◦) occurs at the point where the largest wave elevation
is highest and the positive peak displacement ampliﬁes the surge motion, hence the delay in the
maximum surge peak. This coupling between the surge and pitch motion is also evident in the
pitch PSD plot, where the surge contribution to pitch is seen at the frequency 0.24 Hz. Only minor
energy can be observed at the pitch frequency of 2 Hz.
Figure 4.37 presents the nacelle fore-aft acceleration and mooring line tensions. For better under-
standing of the behaviours the wave elevation is plotted ﬁrst. As expected, the nacelle acceleration
reaches the highest peak at the impact of the largest wave in the focus wave group and is coupled to
the pitching motion. This can also be seen in the free pitch motion where a decay occur after the
wave group has passed the structure and in the corresponding power spectrum where high energy
can be seen at pitch frequency of 2 Hz.
It is seen from the mooring line tensions that the tension amplitudes are in the same magnitude,
which can be explained by the low surge and pitching motion for this sea state. The tension in the
downwind mooring lines achieve lowest loads right after a crest has passed the turbine position
and highest after each trough passing. The opposite is happening to the front mooring line. The
frequency plot shows that the wave frequency is dominating the tension response, with the front
mooring line, number 3, having a moderate energy content at the pitch natural frequency, which
indicates that the line tension, especially for the front mooring line, is inﬂuenced by pitch rather
than surge.

4.5.2
Response to EC8 Extreme Focused Wave
The ﬂoating wind turbine is now exposed to the most severe event tested in the basin, i.e. extreme
focused wave for EC8. Figure 4.38 shows the surge, heave and pitch motion in the EC8 focused
wave. The wave elevation is likewise presented, where the wave peak frequency for EC8 is
fwave = 1/Tp = 0.63 Hz. As in EC5 a short term offset in mean displacement of the surge motion
is observed after the impact of the largest wave. However, the free surge decay after the wave
group has passed the structure appear to be very inﬂuenced by other degrees of freedom. From the
corresponding PSD function a high energy can still be seen at the surge frequency (0.24 Hz) and
the noise below it may be due to the time series not being periodic for the FFT calculations.
The heave response is much less noisy compared to EC5, which is expected due to the large waves.
The second and third surge harmonics captured in the EC5 heave power spectrum can not be seen
in EC8, but now there is much more energy at the wave frequency is present and still some in surge

4.5.3
Controller Comparison with Focused Waves
In this section the performance of the three different control systems in aligned focused waves will
be analysed. As in the previous control sections the labels on the plots onshore and offshore refer to
the controllers tuned for onshore and offshore respectively, whereas ﬁxed is the open loop controller
where a ﬁxed blade pitch and rotational speed is set, according to Table 4.5. The comparison of
the controllers will be carried out based on the response at rated conditions (EC5), but similar
comparison in the highest environmental conditions (EC8) can be found in Appendix F.
Figure 4.40 present the wave elevation η corresponding to the EC 5 focused wave climate and the
surge, heave and pitch response of the structure.
The free surge decay present after the focused wave group has passed the structure is signiﬁcantly
smaller for the ﬁxed controller compared to the case without wind, recall Figure 4.36. This is
due to the aerodynamic damping. The same effect can be seen for the offshore controller, but
77
==PAGE:98==
Results with Extreme Focused Waves
the onshore controller appears to have a signiﬁcantly lower decay ratio. This is likely due to the
aggressive nature of the onshore controller, i.e. it is too sensitive to the changes in torque causing
the controller to react with a too high blade pitch angle. This is also why the onshore controller
achieves the highest surge response to the focused waves and in addition to this, the surge response
is signiﬁcantly stronger before the wave group hits the turbine too, which is due to the turbulent
wind ﬁeld. The aerodynamic damping effect can also be seen in pitch response as the magnitude
for all three controllers are slightly smaller than without wind, recall Figure 4.36.

78

on the front mooring line tension, as it achieves ≈10% higher maximum tension amplitude than

can easily be seen, i.e. the severe reaction to the torque reading, and the response appears to be
governed by the surge frequency and an unknown frequency of 0.65 Hz, which is not visible for the
two other controllers. The offshore controller shows low oscillating blade pitch and is signiﬁcantly
dominated by the surge natural frequency as seen in the corresponding PSD function.

4.5.4
Shutdowns in Focused Waves
In this section the impact of shutdown on the motion of the structure is investigated and the best
way to see the impact is in the extreme events, hence the focused wave groups are used. The
analysis will be conducted with the the offshore controller, and in the environmental case where the
effect of wind is at maximum, hence rated wind speed, EC5. The shutdown of the controller was
79
==PAGE:100==
Results with Extreme Focused Waves
done manually and the aim was to have a shutdown right before the highest crest of the focused
wave group hit the turbine, right after and ﬁnally at the very instant the wave crest breaks at the
turbine position. In the following ﬁgures the labels Before, At and After will refer to where the
shutdown is executed relative to the wave peak. The procedure of the shutdown is so that the pitch
is ramped from the current value to maximum pitch of 50◦and the generator torque set to zero.
Figure 4.42 presents the blade pitch angle θp and rotational speed Ωof the turbine for the shut down
cases of the offshore controller in the EC5 focused wave. Due to the manual shutdown procedure
it was very hard to shutdown the turbine at the exact point of wave impact on the structure. It is
evident from the blade pitch angle signal that the shutdown case at the point where the wave hit the
turbine is actually a bit before. However, it is still considered in the analysis. It is seen from the
rotational speed that the rotor does not reach 0 rpm after a shutdown. This is due to the maximum
pitch angle being 50◦causing an idle state of the turbine with a rotational speed of ≈12 rpm in
EC5.

Figure 4.42: Wave elevation η, blade pitch angle θp and rotational speed Ωfor the shutdown cases

The corresponding motion response, in terms of surge motion, pitch motion, nacelle fore-aft
acceleration and front mooring line tension, can be seen for the three shutdown cases in Figure
4.43. Furthermore, the black dashed lines in the plots represent the focused wave test where no
shutdown was executed. The highest surge response of nearly −0.1m is achieved in the case where
the turbine is shutdown before the wave impact. This can be explained by the incoming trough
forcing the turbine upwind and in the movement the shutdown appears, releasing the thrust force
acting on the rotor, hence the structure experiences a restoring force in the negative x-direction,
resulting in an even greater surge displacement. The opposite effect is seen when the turbine is
shutdown right after the break of the crest, where the surge displacement is damped. Releasing the
thrust force will generate a high pitching moment and for the shutdowns before the wave impact,
two major negative peaks can be observed, hence one from the shutdown and one from the wave
80
==PAGE:101==
peak. Only a slight increase in nacelle fore-aft acceleration can be observed, but the front mooring
line, that is governed by the pitching motion, experiences very low tension when shutdown occur
close to τ3/τ0 = 0.5. A ≈33% decrease of the tension can be observed for shutdown before the
wave impact compared to the case where no shutdown is executed.

4.6
Inverted Waves
Many wave-structure interaction experiments suffer problems with wave reﬂections in ﬁnite sized
wave tanks. This will lead to nonlinear waves and their interaction with a structure results in even
greater nonlinear components of the hydrodynamic loading. The scope in this study, however, is to
analyse the nonlinear part of the incident wave and the resulting force. In order to understand the
physics and quantity of the nonlinear wave loadings a phase separation approach by C. Fitzgerald
[34] was proposed.
By repeating tests with irregular or focused waves and the same wave signal phase shifted π,
resembling an inverted sea state, it is posslible to identify the complete Stokes expansion type
83
==PAGE:104==
Inverted Waves
representation of the applied force: linear, second order two-frequency sum and difference, 3rd
order triple-frequency sum etc. [35].
The method, described by C. Fitzgerald [34], exploits the mathematical structure of Stokes-type
expansion for the hydrodynamic force, and describes the proﬁle in terms of a time-varying incoming
linear wave amplitude a(t) and a force transfer functions f:
F = f11a cos(φ) + f22a2 cos(2φ) + f33a3 cos(3φ) + f44a4 cos(4φ)
+f20a2 + f31a3 cos(φ) + f42a4 cos(2φ)
(4.7)
In the simplest application, the phase separation can distinguish between the even and odd har-
monics through normal crest-focused and trough-focused wave experiments. The trough-wave is
exactly the same wave signal as the crest-wave but inverted and achieved by shifting the phase of
the individual Fourier components of the focusses wave crest signal by π as mentioned above. The
crest-focused and trough-focused waves can then be used to separate into linear, third order, ﬁfth
order etc. and the second order difference, second order sum, fourth order by adding or subtracting
the two signals respectively. This would also allow for the extraction of the ﬁrst four individual
sum harmonics by experiments with Fourier components shifted 0, π/2, π and 3π/2. However,
in this study only the crest- (0◦) and trough-focused (180◦) wave were conducted, hence the two
simple linear combinations of the two phase-shifted runs yield the odd and even harmonics [34]:
(F0 −F180)
2
= f11a cos(φ) + f31a3 cos(φ) + f33a3 cos(3φ) + O(a5)
(4.8)
(F0 + F180)
2
= f20a2 + f22a2 cos(2φ) + f42a4 cos(2φ) + f44a4 cos(4φ) + O(a6)
(4.9)
In this analysis the phase separation method will be used for investigating the long focused wave
tests in terms of their time series, where only wave forcing is present. However, the method will
also be applied on the long irregular wave tests, but here the analysis will focus on the Power
Spectral Density (PSD) functions.
4.6.1
Extreme Focused Waves
Long crest- and trough-focused wave group tests were conducted for each environmental condition,
recall Figure 3.1, consisting of eight peak groups in each test and a full duration of 960 s, see an
example in Figure 4.46. It should also be noted that the analysis is based on the measurements from
wave gauge nr. 8. An approach of extracting a single representative time series by combining all
eight peak groups is adopted for both crest and trough before using the phase separation method.
Firstly the time series are cut up in segments around each peak group and then the crest and trough
are aligned respectively so that the largest crests and troughs are located at t = 0, as shown for
EC6 in Figure 4.47. The other sea states can be seen in Appendix G. An element-wise average
of the time history segments were computed and used for further analysis. The result is a single
reconstructed signal derived from the crest and trough of the signal, F0 and F180 respectively. In
this case the wave elevation is used, hence η0 and η180. The method of phase separation, equation
(4.8) and (4.9), is used on the reconstructed wave elevation histories η0 and η180 in order to obtain
84
==PAGE:105==

for both normal (crest) and inverted (trough) test. Bottom left: reconstructed wave elevation
histories for crest-focused wave groups. Bottom right: reconstructed wave elevation histories for
trough-focused wave groups.
the odd η[1] and even η[2] harmonics, see Figure 4.48a. However, the same methodology was
applied on the surge response of the structure and the nacelle fore-aft acceleration, Figure 4.48b
and 4.48c. Here the odd order harmonics are noted with a [1] and the even order harmonics with
a [2]. As expected the contribution from second order (and fourth order) harmonics in the wave
elevation increase with larger environmental conditions, since the waves become more nonlinear.
However comparing the magnitude of the linear and third order harmonics with the even-order
harmonics (second, fourth, etc.) the linear region is dominating for all ECs. Also, compared with
the linear surge responses, the same tendencies as for the linear wave elevations can be observed,
but for the even order differences, the surge motion is suddenly governed by its natural frequency
of 0.24 Hz. Furthermore, the magnitude of the even order differences on the surge motion is much
larger than the ones seen in the wave elevation or nacelle acceleration.

(a) Wave elevation
(b) Surge
(c) Nacelle fore-aft acceleration
Figure 4.48: Wave elevation, surge response and nacelle fore-aft acceleration decomposed into
odd-, noted [1], and even-, noted [2], harmonics.

4.6.2
Irregular Waves
In continuation of long focused waves, long irregular wave experiments were conducted for both
crest (0◦) and trough (180◦) each with a full duration of 46 minutes. An example of the irregular
crest and corresponding inverted wave signal can be seen in Figure 4.49. Due to the random nature
of irregular waves, the analysis will be based on the Power Spectral Density (PSD) functions
contrary to time series as done for the focused wave.
1000
1005
1010
1015
1020
1025
1030
1035
1040
Time [s]
-0.1
-0.05
0
0.05
0.1
0.15
η [m]
EC6
Crest (0 deg)
Trough (180 deg)
Figure 4.49: An example of wave elevation in long duration tests with irregular waves both with
normal crest signal and with inverted signal (Trough).
Here the phase separation method, equation (4.8) and (4.9), is again used on the wave elevation,
surge and nacelle fore-aft acceleration to extract the odd and even order harmonics, but now their
corresponding PSD functions are calculated. The results can be seen in Figure 4.50-4.52. When
looking at the wave elevation, one would expect to see an increased contribution from second
order in the highest environmental conditions in terms of peaks in the sub- and higher-harmonics.
However, these effects are not pronounced for the conducted tests and it may be due to very small
nonlinear wave effects at the large water depth. On the other hand, when looking at the surge PSD,
a huge impact from second order effects can be observed at the surge natural frequency for all
environmental conditions, which was also seen in the previous focused wave analysis. Thus, even
though the nonlinear part of the wave elevation seems to be small at the surge natural frequency,
it has a large inﬂuence on the response. Also, small 2nd order effects are visible on the nacelle
acceleration at the pitch natural frequency of the system 2 Hz.
It can be concluded that the analysis done with both the focused and irregular wave tests demonstrate
the importance of the nonlinear components in the description of extreme wave loadings.

CHAPTER 5
Numerical TLP Wind Turbine Model
Design
This chapter will deal with the implementation of the numerical models in the two aero-hydro-servo-
elastic codes; HAWC2 and FAST. This will include the a description of the codes, the input ﬁles
and main model parameters needed for the two codes to work. Moreover the WAMIT model and
simulation setup will be presented together with the result ﬁles going into the two numerical models.
Next, the process of calibrating the models will be presented through comparison of key features,
such as the ﬁxed tower decay, steady thrust curve and free decay tests in water. Furthermore the
model control implementation and tuning will be described and lastly the models are validated by
comparing the dynamic response to regular waves with the experimental results.
5.1
Model Parameters
The main structural parameters are listed in Section 2.4.3 providing dimensions and masses of the
complete ﬂoater and turbine. Yet, in order to model the system a few parameters more had to be
calculated, shown in Table 5.1. Mooring line stiffness were experimentally determined as explained
in Section 5.1.1 and used as an input for the mooring models. For the structural modelling of the
ﬂoater in FAST and HAWC2 the center of gravity and moment of inertia were obtained in the CAD
software SOLIDWORKS were a model of the ﬂoater was drawn. The buoyancy force was taken
as an output from WAMIT. The drag coefﬁcients for the implementation of non viscous drag in
the numerical models were taken from [20, ch. 4] based on calculated Re and KC number for the
different sea states.

5.1.1
Mooring Line Properties
For the model tests a Dyneema® ﬁber rope with diameter of 2.2 mm and breaking load of around
990 kg, where used as mooring lines, due to its high tensile strength to weight ratio. The numerical
models require the stiffness of the mooring lines as an input, hence a simple Hooke’s experiment
was set up, where the elongation of the rope was measured when loading it with different known
masses ranging from 0-30 kg. The setup can be seen in Figure 5.1(a) and 5.1(b), where a pre-load
of 3.1 kg on the rope was applied, seen as the aluminium ring, to ensure that the rope was loaded at
all time. The elongation was then measured with a micrometre, ﬁxated on an external plate and
with measuring point at the bottom of the aluminium ring. The length of the rope for the setup was
measured to 7.0 m, the uncertainty of the micrometre measurements was ±0.3 mm and the weight
of the cable itself was measured to 4.615 · 10−3 kg/m.
(a)
(b)

σ [Pa]
×107
Loading - E = 2.67e+10 Pa

Figure 5.1: Left and middle: simple setup for Hooke’s experiment. Right: rope stress σ as function
of elongation ϵ.
90
==PAGE:111==
Hooke’s law states that the strain of the rope is proportional to the stress and is given as:
σ = Eϵ
(5.1)
where σ is the tensile stress, ϵ is the tensile strain calculated as the change in length per unit length
and E is Young’s modulus describing the ratio of the unit stress to the unit strain, i.e. the elasticity.
The rope stress as function of elongation is shown in Figure 5.1(c) and the Young’s modulus of
the mooring line is computed as the slope of a linear regression ﬁtted to the loading and unloading
data. An average of the two slopes was then used to estimate a combined E for the mooring lines
after the stiffness of the rope is computed as the elasticity times the cross-sectional area, i.e. EA.
The stiffness of the mooring lines was computed to EAmoor = 97.86 · 103 N.
5.2
Hydrodynamic input - WAMIT
This section provides a brief description of WAMIT and the setup which has been used to provide
the radiation/diffraction coefﬁcients for the numerical models; FAST and HAWC2. A deﬁnition
of the quantities as well as an example of an output from WAMIT is given. The theory behind
WAMIT is presented in Appendix C. WAMIT is a panel method solver for radiation/diffraction
loads based on potential ﬂow formulation of the ﬂow ﬁeld for analysing the interaction of plane
progressive waves with one or multiple interacting offshore structures in ﬁnite or inﬁnite depth.
5.2.1
Hydrodynamic quantities from WAMIT
In this project, emphasis has been on the added-mass, damping, excitation forces and the hydrostatic
matrix which are inputs to the FAST and HAWC2 models. WAMIT output these quantities in
non-dimensional form. The hydrostatic coefﬁcients are calculated by surface integrals over the
mean body wetted surface, through application of Gauss’ divergence theorem [36, ch. 3-3]. The
hydrostatic restoring force C(i,j), is given in the non-dimensional form,
¯C(i,j) = C(i, j)
ρ g Ln ,
(5.2)
where (i, j) denotes the mode indices (matrix index), and n is a power to the length scale, L,
dependent on the index, (i, j). ρ is the water density and g is the acceleration of gravity.
The added mass, A, and damping, B coefﬁcients are calculated by (5.3) [36], where the right hand
side represents the complex force in i’th direction due to a sinusoidal motion of unit amplitude in
the j’th direction [21].

(5.3)
The added mass and damping coefﬁcients are non-dimensionalised as shown in Eq. (5.4)

Hydrodynamic input - WAMIT
The excitation forces, Xi are, in this projects calculated by direct integration of hydrodynamic
pressure from the diffraction potential. It consists of two components, the Froude-Krylov force and
scattering, respectively. [36, ch. 3-4]

5.2.2
Simulation Setup
WAMIT consists of two principal sub programs, POTEN and FORCE. POTEN solves for the
velocity potentials, i.e., radiation and diffraction. Whereas FORCE computes the motions of the
body, ﬂuid pressure, velocity, hydrodynamic stiffness, added mass and damping of the body [36].
A number of input ﬁles are required to set up WAMIT and control the simulation. The three most
important input ﬁles will be brieﬂy introduced and includes information about the structure, and
control ﬁles for POTEN and FORCE. For more information please see [36]. The output ﬁles from
the simulation is presented in Section 5.2.3. Two WAMIT simulation has been run, one for FAST
and HAWC2, since the coordinate system in HAWC is mirrored as seen in Figure 5.6. Hence the
geometry of the ﬂoater needed to be mirrored.
Geometric Data File (.gdf)
In this project the Low-Order method has been used to discretise the surface of the ﬂoater. This
implies that the wetted surface is divided into a number of connected panels on where the velocity
potential is solved for. Thus the Geometric Data File contains the coordinate of all the panel
vertices as well as information on symmetry planes, gravity and length scale. Given the ﬂoater
symmetry around x-axis, y = 0 was set as a symmetry plane. 2016 panels were used to discretise
the symmetric half-body.
Due to the complex geometry of the ﬂoater with square spokes that attaches to the main ﬂoater
body, it was deemed too difﬁcult to create a simple script in MATLAB that could provide a tight
connection between spokes and main body of the ﬂoater. Further, the size of the spokes implied that
they should be included. Therefore, it was decided to draw model of the ﬂoater in MultiSurf [37].
Multisurf is a CAD tool with the ability to create freeform surface shapes and export a Geometric
Data File to WAMIT. The drawn model and the imported panels are shown in Figure 5.2.
92
==PAGE:113==
(a)
(b)
Figure 5.2: (a): Full model in MultiSurf. (b) Floater geometry seen by Wamit
Potential Control File (.pot)
The Potential Control File contains the input settings for the POTEN sub program. Among these,
the water depth, h, wave periods, T and wave heading angles, β. For this project, the wave depth
was set to inﬁnite, to imitate deep water. A number of wave periods in the interval from 0.15 s to
50 s was applied. The wave heading angles, β, was in the interval −180◦to 180◦in steps of 10◦in
order to resolve proper excitation forces for all degrees of freedom.
Force Control File (.frc)
The Force Control File holds the input parameters to the FORCE sub program. It is here set which
hydrodynamic parameters that should be evaluated and given as output. The parameters are for
example calculation of added mass and damping, exciting forces, radii of gyration and vertical
center of gravity. Further, points in ﬂuid domain to output i.e. hydrodynamic pressure and external
mass and stiffness matrices may be speciﬁed.
5.2.3
WAMIT output
Based on the analysis of ﬂow regimes in Section 2.3.2, the primary hydrodynamic forcing were
computed with WAMIT providing information of added mass, damping and exciting forces. In
Figure 5.3 these quantities are presented for surge, heave and pitch in their dimensional form.
However, the wave excitation forces, Xi, remains normalized by the incoming wave amplitude, a,

5.3
Numerical Model Implementation
The implementation of the numerical model in HAWC2 and FAST will be presented in this section.
Both tools are aero-servo-elastic codes, which means they are able to simulate the behaviour of a
wind turbine considering wind loads, the structure dynamics and the control system.
For the present work two previous models, i.e. one for HAWC and one for FAST, were used for the
downscaled DTU reference wind turbine mounted on a four spoke TLP tested in 2015 [9]. The
scaled models were constructed by A. Kocaturk [13] and A. Jurado as part of their master thesis
and ongoing PhD work respectively. Both models were adapted to match the new model used in
the experiments, recall speciﬁcations in section 2.4.3.
5.3.1
HAWC2
HAWC2 (Horizontal Axis Wind turbine simulation Code 2nd generation) is an aeroelastic code
intended for calculating the response of onshore, bottom mounted or ﬂoating offshore Horizontal
Axis Wind Turbines in the time domain [38]. Since the structure of investigation will be ﬂoating
several other loads and models should be considered, as seen in Figure 5.4.
94
==PAGE:115==
Figure 5.4: The structure of HAWC2 code. Source [39]
The HAWC2 solver operates though a main .htc ﬁle, where all components are modelled, oriented
and constrained, and where the corresponding input ﬁles and external DLLs are called. The global
coordinate system of HAWC2 is oriented, as shown in Figure 5.5, where the origin is located
at the mean water level (MWL), thus in the middle of the transition piece. In order to easily be
able to compare the results from HAWC2 with the FAST simulations the output from HAWC2 is
transformed into the global coordinate system deﬁned for this project, recall Figure 2.1.
Aerodynamic modelling
Aerodynamic modeling in HAWC2 uses the classic Blade Element Momentum (BEM) theory
approach to model the aerodynamic forces acting on the blade [38]. The aerodynamic forces acting
on the structure includes the forces acting on the blades as well the aerodynamic drag forces acting
on the tower and the nacelle. The forces are transferred to the structural model in order to determine
the aero-elastic response as described in B.1. The aerodynamic input for HAWC2 consists of two
ﬁles and a center line deﬁnition in the main .htc ﬁle. The ﬁrst one being a .ae data ﬁle containing the
geometric characteristics of the airfoil sections, i.e. span-wise position, chord length and relative
thickness, and the second being a .pc data ﬁle containing the aerodynamic properties of the airfoils,
i.e. lift, drag and moment coefﬁcients of the airfoils as function of angle of attack. Since the blades
has not been changed the data ﬁles and the blade bodies were kept the same as in [13] and are
based on the work of the aerodynamic downscaling of the DTU 10 MW wind turbine blade for
model testing of 1:60 scale [40].
The aerodynamic modeling in HAWC2 is based on the unsteady BEM model with several cor-
rections and models implemented in order to account for dynamic or skewed inﬂow, tower shear
effects, dynamic stall, etc. The procedure of the BEM model is implemented by discretizing the
rotor plane into independent concentric annular elements and azimuthal variation of loading, as
described in section 2.3.1.
95
==PAGE:116==
Numerical Model Implementation
Structural modelling
Aeroelastic simulations consist of an aerodynamic and a structural part. The main structural
components such as the tower, the nacelle, the bearings, the main shaft, the hub and the blades
has to be structural modelled for the aeroelastic analysis of the turbine. The dynamics of the wind
turbine can be understood by the structural modelling of the HAWT in multibody formulation
[41]. Meaning that the wind turbine structure is divided into a number of main bodies consisting
of a number of elements, where each element is considered a 6 degree of freedom Timoshenko
beam element which accounts for both shear and bending deformation. Each body has its own
local coordinate system and the calculations of the body inertial loads are based on the speciﬁc
body coordinate system when the body is moved in space. The main bodies, representing the main
components of the turbine, are connected by joints, referred to as constraints, which are formulated
as constraint equations that impose limitations in the motion of the bodies. The capability of
modelling ﬂoating turbines is inherent in the general multi-body formulation, which does not need
to have the modelled structure attached to the global frame of reference [41].
The structural input for HAWC2 consists of a .st data ﬁle for each structural component containing
19 structural parameters for each element the main body is divided into. These parameters consist
of structural stiffness and damping properties and the full list can be seen in [38]. Furthermore a
center line in the local body coordinate system is deﬁned in the main .htc ﬁle for each main body.
The orientation of each body is also speciﬁed, regarding the position and rotation, thus linking the
local coordinate system to the global coordinate system.
Wind and turbulence modelling
The stochastic wind is generated outside the HAWC2 code. The turbulence generator is the Mann
model [38], which is a full 3D correlated turbulence ﬂow ﬁeld. Tower shadow effects are also a
part of the wind module, as it changes the wind conditions locally near the tower.
The wind is deﬁned at the centre of the hub, 1.87 m in height from the still water level. The box
covers an area of 2.98 m × 2.98m in the rotor plane with center at the hub height. The total length
of the box along the direction of the wind has to be in accordance with the simulation length, see
5.2 for the used lengths of the Mann box in the simulations. All simulations were conducted with
a turbulence intensity of TI = 3.5% and an example of the time series of wind speed at the hub
for the irregular wave simulations can be seen in Figure 5.5. Due to time limitations only one
turbulence seed was used in the numerical simulations.
Lengths of the Mann box

Mooring system modelling
The mooring system is modelled in HAWC2 by an external system DLL (ESYSMooring) and called
by the HAWC2 solver during the simulation. The developed dynamic mooring line model is based
on a general cable element formulation taking into account hydrodynamic drag and buoyancy
forces of the cable and a nonlinear spring stiffness are included to account for bottom contact [41].
Furthermore the element formulation only holds for cables with uniform properties.
The mooring model is implemented into HAWC2 by including two blocks in the main .htc ﬁle
and they enable externally formulated equations of motion to be solved together with the core
equations of motion in the HAWC2 code [41]. In the ﬁrst block the mooring lines are constructed
based on 10 user deﬁned parameters, including the number of elements in the mooring line, mass,
anchor and fairlead coordinates, drag and added mass coefﬁcients, axial stiffness of the line and the
bottom properties. The second block interacts with the HAWC2 model through a set of constraint
equations with purpose of linking the mooring line to the bottom and the ﬂoating wind turbine.
Hydrodynamics
In the HAWC2 code the Morison formula is included in order to calculate the wave loading on the
offshore turbine, as a supplements to the outputs from WAMIT ,as input to HAWC2, as described
in section 2.3.2. A standalone coupling to WAMIT has been developed [42], where the external
DLL interface in HAWC2 is used to read the WAMIT output ﬁles directly and input to the HAWC2-
WAMIT external module, ESYSWamit. The wave input to ESYSWamit is taken directly from the
wave deﬁnition built into HAWC2 and used to lookup and transform the radiation and diffraction
forces in order to model the loads in six degrees of freedom of the rigid body ﬂoating structure.
97
==PAGE:118==
Numerical Model Implementation
The inputs to the ESYSWamit module include the center of gravity, the buoyancy and the radius
of gyration of the combined submerged body. The necessary constraints are implemented within
the ESYSWamit It should be noted that the ESYSWamit module uses a coordinate system opposite
to the one used in HAWC, as seen in Figure 5.6, and should be kept in mind when specifying the
input parameters.
Figure 5.6: HAWC2 coordinate system and ESYSWamit coordinate system.
The forces from the WAMIT output ﬁles does not include viscous forces, hence the internal HAWC2
Morison formulation is used to only to include the Morison drag term. This is accomplished
modelling the submerged bodies in main .htc ﬁle, i.e. the transition piece, ﬂoater body and spokes,
as done for the main structural components. However they are modelled as "dummy" bodies,
meaning stiff massless bodies with no buoyancy. The estimated drag coefﬁcients applied to these
submerged bodies and used in the simulations can be seen Table 5.1.
5.3.2
FAST
FAST (Fatigue, Aerodynamics, Structures and Turbulence) is an aero-hydro-servo-elastic model in
time-domain developed at NREL, National Renewable Energy Laboratory, by Jonkman and Buhl
[43]. It is written for analysis of two and three bladed horizontal axis wind turbines and is available
as an open source software. An overview of the modularized FAST version v8.16.00a-bjj used in
this project is shown in Figure 5.7 [44]. The modules used in this project and their version is listed
in table 5.3
98
==PAGE:119==
Module

Table 5.3: overview and versionlist of the modules used in the FAST simulations
Figure 5.7: Overview of the modular framework of FAST v8.16.00a-bjj [44]
Aerodynamics
The aerodynamic loading is computed by Blade Element Momentum (BEM) theory with empircal
modiﬁcations as explained in [45]. The airfoil data is the same as explained in the Aerodynamics
section of HAWC2 in 5.3.1. In FAST, the Beddoes-Leishman dynamic stall model was enabled and
the implementation of this model is of courtesy to PhD student Antonio M.P. Jurado. Tower shadow
was not included since the tower potential ﬂow model is ﬁxed at its initial position in chosen
version of AeroDyn [46]. The blades were modelled as stiff. The wind input for the simulations
was imported through the InﬂowWind module. Attempts of importing the turbulence boxes created
in HAWC2 were not successful and thus the recoded time history of the wind created in HAWC2
was imported as an uniform proﬁle due to no signiﬁcant shear effects in the measured physical ﬂow.
ServoDyn was active when simulating cases with active control. The implementation of control is
presented in Section 5.5.
Structural
The structural part of the modelling is performed in the ElastoDyn module. The ElastoDyn model
is written as a combination of modal-dynamics- and multi-body formulation based on Bernoulli-
Euler beams. Therefore, ﬁrst and second mode shape corresponding to ﬁrst and second natural
frequency of the tower has been calculated. With the tower ﬁxed, the tower stiffness were tuned
to match experimental tower decay tests. Subsequently, the tower was "mounted on the ﬂoater"
with calculated external stiffness and hydrostatics to calculate the correct mode shapes (sixth order
99
==PAGE:120==
Numerical Model Implementation
polynomial). This procedure was carried out in BModes [47] and by use of curveﬁtting in MATLAB.
The ﬂoater and blades were modelled as rigid bodies.
Hydrodynamics
The computation of wave kinematics and hydrodynamic forcing is carried out in the HydroDyn
module [48]. For this project the module is fed with the measured wave elevation and calculates
the linear kinematics internally. A hybrid approach to compute the hydrodynamic loads has been
chosen which includes both the radiation and diffraction loads imported from WAMIT and a
strip-theory approach for the inclusion of nonlinear viscous drag. The strip-theory method is
applied for all submerged members. The development and implementation of the hydrodynamics
module is given in [49].
Mooring system
The FAST modularization framework allows for three different mooring models; (1) Moordyn, an
open-source lumped mass mooring line model, (2) FEAMooring, a ﬁnite element model based on
an elastic rod model and (3) MAP++, an analytical nonlinear quasi-static formulation. Sudden
numerical instabilities was observed when the implemented mooring model was (1). Different
solution strategies was proposed, however, the root of the problem was not solved within this
project. Subsequently, the performance of (2) was investigated with decent results, but, changing
the mooring line stiffness revealed that the model did not behave as expected. Thus, the simplest
quasi-static mooring line module MAP++ [50] was chosen for this project despite it was shown
in [51] that MAP++ might only predict the mean load and not the amplitude of the line tension
correctly. On the other hand, it was found that the platform motions can be well predicted which
was deemed more important for this project. The quasi-static approach does not take dynamic
line loads i.e. structural inertia or hydrodynamic loads nor does it take line bending stiffness into
account.
5.3.3
Wave input for both models
During the experiments, the wave elevation was recorded at the positions shown in Figure 3.1.
For use in the numerical simulations the measurements from wave gauge 8 (WG8) has been used.
Together with WG9 it was placed next to the turbine with the same distance to the wave maker.
The measurements from WG8 has been compared with WG9 in order to avoid calibration errors.
Subsequently the measurements of aligned waves were directly imported as input to the numerical
models. Obtaining the wave elevation signal for misaligned waves required a small workaround
as measurements from the location of the turbine was not possible. However, knowing that the
oblique angle of the waves were 30◦and the lateral distance form the turbine to WG8 was ≈2m
the measured wave elevation was translated to the position of the turbine. An actual distance of
1.9 m was used in the computations as it matched the phase in the motion signals. A brief walk
through of the method is presented here. Knowing that a wave elevation of an irregular wave can
100
==PAGE:121==
be written as,
η =

ajei(ωjt−kjχ)
(5.8)
and transforming it to the frequency domain only the location χ is explicit, thus the measured wave
elevation and the wave elevation at the turbine position in the frequency domain is written as
ˆηj,WG8 = ˆaje−i(kjχW G8)
(5.9)

where the distance χWG8 −χTurbine = 1.9˙sin 30◦is derived from simple trigonometry. By
applying Inverse Fourier Transformation (IFFT) to (5.12) the wave elevation signal in time domain
is obtained at the position of the turbine.
5.4
Model Calibration
A model of the scaled turbine and ﬂoater has been set up in both FAST and HAWC2. Next, a static
equilibrium test, match of aerodynamic thrust and decay tests were performed and are presented
here.
5.4.1
Static equilibrium
The transition piece for the TLP model was designed with a length of 0.3 m, with the mean water
level (MWL) located at the middle, thus only 0.15 m between MWL and the tower bottom. This
meant that for the highest sea state with a signiﬁcant wave height of Hs = 0.167 m, the amplitude
of largest expected wave would be amax = Hs · 1.86/2 = 0.155 m, and thus the tower bottom
would be included in the water column. Unfortunately, the cable outlet from the tower was located
at the tower bottom and even though sealed with tape, as seen in Figure 3.9, the ﬂoater took in
water. Luckily the transition piece and the ﬂoater body were two individual parts, thus only the
transition piece was ﬁlled with water at the end of the test campaign. The ﬂoater had a dry weight
of 16.5 kg before it was put into the water. Subsequently to ﬁnishing the tests, the ﬂoater showed
a weight of 19.0 kg measured at the last day of the test campaign. The effect of the water intake
could be seen in the measured pretension in the mooring lines over the main production days of
the test campaign, as seen in Figure 5.8. It should be noted that draft would of course change due
to the water intake, but the tension in the mooring line were adjusted each day to ensure that the
MWL was located at the middle of the transition piece. Furthermore the draft of the TLP model
could not be lowered since otherwise the top of the ﬂoater body would be exposed in the higher sea
101
==PAGE:122==
Model Calibration
states leading to severe response as the ﬂoater would loose its buoyancy.
The pretension before the production tests were started, was measured from the Z-gauges to ≈89.3
N and the water intake measured after the test campaign meant that each mooring line had ≈7.6 N
less tension than in the beginning, corresponding to a total weight reduction of 2.32 kg and agrees
with the measured weight of the ﬂoater with water.
Figure 5.8: Historic of the mean mooring line tension for each production day.
The next step is to validate the pretension in the mooring lines for the case where no water was
present in the transition piece. Both HAWC and FAST reaches the same result with a pretension of
96.73 N, which is far from the 89.3 N measured pretension in the beginning of the test campaign.
In order to conclude whether the models or the Z-gauges are off, the static conditions with no wave
and no wind is considered. The tension in each of the three mooring lines in equilibrium position
can be determined by using equation (2.12):
τ0 = FB −FG
3
= g(ρwaterVsubmerged −mtot)
3
(5.13)
where g is the gravitational constant, ρwater is the density of water and Vsubmerged = 51.8·10−3 m3
is the volume of the submerged bodies, i.e. spokes, ﬂoater body and half of the transition piece,
and is taken from the WAMIT output ﬁle. With g = 9.82 m/s2, ρwater = 1000 kg/m3 and the
total mass of the system mtot as measured in Section 2.4.3, the tension in each mooring line can
be estimated to τ0 = 96.740 N, which agrees nicely with the numerical models. This means that
something went wrong with the Z-gauges and as mentioned in the Section 3.4 the difference is
likely due to the Z-gauges ﬂoating a bit in their calibration.
In order to be able to compare the experimental results it was then decided to normalise the
experimental mooring line tensions based on their corresponding daily pretension.

5.4.2
Tower Decay
Tower decay tests have been performed subsequent to the KIER-DTU test campaign as it became
clear that the nacelle mass had changed by nearly 0.6 kg due to removal of a gear and the replace-
ment of the two servomotors. The tower decay test was carried out by mounting the turbine on a
stiff plate on the ﬂoor and then displacing the nacelle from its equilibrium position before releasing
it. The response was measured by a 6DOF accelerometer, yielding a damped natural frequency and
damping ratio shown in Table 5.4.

The tower decay tests were carried out in FAST and HAWC2 by locking the degrees of freedom for
the platform, mimicking the conditions in the experimental test. In FAST an initial displacement
was introduced at the tower top. In HAWC2 a force was applied at the tower top in order to displace
the nacelle before releasing it. The tower fore-aft natural frequency has been matched by tuning the
stiffness of the tower. This was done by adjusting the Young’s modulus of the material. The results
from the numerical decay tests are shown in table 5.5 and Table 5.6. With an uniform Young’s
modulus, it was not possible to match the measured tower side-side frequency. However, HAWC2,
depicts the same tendency, which also was discovered by [9]. This discrepancy was not investigated
further, as the fore-aft motions were deemed more important.

5.4.3
Rotor Thrust
In continuation of Section 4.1.3, where the concept and importance of matching the aerodynamic
thrust, as well as how it was carried out, is discussed; this section concerns the calibration of the
numerical models. Similar to the experimental test, the turbine is rigidly mounted to a non-moving
foundation. Due to the very large stiffness of the physical blades used in the experiments, they are
modelled as rigid in FAST and HAWC2. The numerical models are run based on the identiﬁed
pitch settings from the experiments that will yield the scaled thrust, as well as the downscaled rotor
speed, see Section 4.1.3. Likewise, the wind ﬁeld is similar with a turbulence intensity of 3.5 %.
The results are shown in the left column of Figure 5.9. At low and highest wind speeds the deviation
between measured and computed thrust is rather small. However, at wind speeds around rated
wind speed, deviations between measured and computed thrust as well as differences between the
two models are visible. The differences between the two numerical models are most likely due to
differences in the implementation of the unsteady BEM-modiﬁcations in the two models.

In the tuning process, the thrust has been tuned to match the one in the experiments rather than

numerical models are to be compared to the experimental data and not models based on theory.
Looking at the right hand column in Figure 5.9, the blade pitch is adjusted in the two numerical
models to represent the measured thrust within 1 % deviation. The differences between the two
models, discussed above is now seen in the different blade pitch settings. Compared with the
experiment, the new pitch settings in the numerical models are within 1.2◦.
5.4.4

Decay tests in six degrees of freedom were carried out in the basin at DHI as explained in Section

not be avoided completely. This made the tendons act as a non-linear spring. In addition, a clear
response in the frequency spectrum was not present. Hence, it is listed in brackets and with great
caution. When looking into the data from the decay test in roll, the response appeared to be ﬁlled
with noise from other degrees of freedom. Therefore,it is not possible to identify a damping ratio
for this degree of freedom.

The Decay test similar to the ones carried out experimentally, has been carried out in FAST, by

thereby pure WAMIT-FAST or WAMIT-HAWC2 coupling that is presented. Both models produce
a perfect match in the degrees of freedom surge and sway. Regarding heave, both numerical models
depicts a natural frequency of 5.99 Hz. This implies that the experimentally depicted heave natural
frequency is incorrect. The difference in heave between the numerical model and experiments is
not investigated further, taking the difﬁculty of performing the heave decay test into account. The
damped natural frequency in yaw is close to perfect in FAST, whereas HAWC2 shows a difference
of 8 % compared to the frequency obtained in the experiment. However, further effort has not been
put into matching the natural frequency in this degree of freedom.

Whereas HAWC2 and FAST is capable of producing the natural frequencies in most DOF, roll and
pitch reveals a large discrepancy of 16 % is seen in pitch and roll for FAST and even higher in roll
for HAWC2. A surprising difference which have been tried clariﬁed through an investigation of
what inﬂuences the system pitch natural frequency. Pitch was chosen since in-plane motions are
the most crucial for majority of the analysis carried out in this report. Besides the nature of the two
modes is similar. Considering system pitch, the largest inﬂuencers on the natural frequency will,
besides tower top mass, be tower stiffness and tendon stiffness. Changing the model parameters
mentioned above affects the system pitch natural frequency which is shown in PSD plots presented
106
==PAGE:127==
in Figure 5.10. This ﬁgure serves as a sensitivity study towards what inﬂuences the system pitch
natural frequency. The ﬁrst row shows the system pitch natural frequency as computed by FAST
and HAWC2. The second plot shows the isolated tower frequency in fore-aft and the third plot
shows the system pitch with the tower modelled as rigid. The black dashed line in all plots is the
pitch frequency from free decay tests in the experiments. Next, two examples of how the system
pitch natural frequency will be if the tower stiffness is doubled or reduced by half. Similarly with
changing the stiffness of the mooring lines.
Evaluating the results in Figure 5.10, it is seen that HAWC2 computes two natural frequencies
from the decay test. The mode shapes corresponding to the two peaks were investigated through
eigenvalues analysis in HAWC2 and animated. Based on that, it was concluded that the peak at
2.03 Hz is caused by the mooring cables, most likely a higher order mooring cable mode forcing.
The other peak at 2.35 Hz showed to be linked to the tower and is taken to be the system natural
frequency. A change in tower stiffness of 23 % would be necessary in order to match the system
natural frequency in pitch. However, the tower stiffness is tuned in order to match the measured
isolated tower frequencies from decay tests performed with a ﬁxed tower on land. Hence, it is not
desirable to adjust this parameter, as the conﬁdence level for the tower free decay results is high.
In addition, the mass of the tower and tower top (blades, hub, nacelle) has been measured, and is
therefore known with high precision. Taking into account the peak frequencies of the sea states
ranging from 0.63 Hz for EC8 to 1.19 Hz for EC3 wave forcing frequencies are much lower. In
addition, 1P and 3P frequencies are also out of range. Thus, the system pitch natural frequency of
2.32 Hz and 2.35 Hz computed by FAST and HAWC2, respectively, will not be sought calibrated
further to match the measured frequency of 2.0 Hz.

5.5
Controller Setup
All the parameters of the controller are transferred to the Dynamic Link Library (DLL) using
HAWC2 commands for the initialisation routine. The DLL interface for the controller is the
type2_dll which is an updated, slightly modiﬁed version of the previous hawc_dll interface
[38]. In the Type2 DLL format a subroutine within an externally written DLL is setup. This
subroutine calls two, one-dimensional arrays are transferred between the HAWC2 core and the DLL
procedure. The ﬁrst contains data going from the HAWC2 core to the DLL and the second contains
data going from the DLL to the core. It is very important to notice that the data are transferred
between HAWC2 and the DLL in the ﬁrst call of every timestep, where the out-going variables are
based on last iterated values from previous time step [38].
In the dll connected with using the Type2 DLL interface, two subroutines should be present. An
initialization routine called only once before the time simulation begins and an update routine
called in every time step. The format in the calling of these two subroutines are identical, where
two arrays of double precision is exchanged as described in [38].
As input during simulations, the controller requires the time [s], the generator LSS speed [rad/s],
the pitch angles of three blades [rad] and the three components of the wind speed at hub height
[m/s], where the ﬁrst and second of these three components must be the two horizontal components
that are used internally to compute the horizontal vector sum.
The DLL ﬁle containing the Basic DTU Wind Energy Controller is implemented for use in FAST
by utilizing a wrapper. Researcher Mahmood Mirzaei at DTU Wind Energy was kind to help
implement the wrapper. The wrapper interfaces the control DLL to a Bladed-style DLL, which
FAST is able to read. The controller settings are speciﬁed in a separate input ﬁle. For more
information on how to adapt DTU Basic DTU Wind Energy Controller through bladed-style DLL,
please refer to [53].
Initial tests done in HAWC2 of the controller based on the input parameters from the experiments
showed that it was not possible to run with exactly the same controller setup in the numerical
models as in the experiments, mainly due to the high gearbox friction. The full-load controller was
robust against errors in the initial blade pitch (DC errors) and only minor tuning of the experimental
PI pitch gains were needed, which was done by trial and error. However, the partial-load controller
needed some more treatment as it was not operating on optimum values. In order to estimate the
input parameters for the model partial-load controller several open loop step tests were conducted,
i.e. simulations with speciﬁc blade pitch, ﬁxed rotational speed and steps in wind speed. The
simulations were done within the operational range, meaning blade pitch θp = [−2; 13] deg,
rotational speed Ω= [5.13; 7.79] rad/s and wind speed V = [1.0; 2.3] m/s with the hydro-dynamic
modules disabled. In order to ensure the wind turbine is operating on the optimum, power coefﬁcient
CP,max was calculated for each simulation based on the power output of the turbine, recall equation
(2.26). Since the power coefﬁcient is dependent on the tip-speed ratio (TSR) and the blade pitch
angle, a surface plot of CP (λ, θp) could be constructed and the results can be seen in Figure 5.11.
109
==PAGE:130==
Controller Setup
Figure 5.11: Aerodynamic power coefﬁcient Cp as function of TSR λ and blade pitch θp for the
TLP Wind Turbine model.
The inputs to the partial-load controller would be the factor,K in the K −Ωscheme, when operating
at minimum rotational speed and the PI gains for the torque control (KPg and KIg) when operating
in variable speed, as described in section 2.5.1. The K parameter, ensuring that the turbine is
operating on optimum conditions, can be estimated through equation (2.28):
K = 1
2
ρAR3CP,max
λ3
opt
(5.17)
where the optimum conditions of the turbine, i.e. CP,max = CP (λopt, θp,opt), can be found as the
TSR and the blade pitch resulting in the maximum obtainable power of the simulations. Based on
the simulations the optimum condition was found to be around CP,max = CP (7.585, 0◦) = 0.396.
The corresponding K-value can be seen in Table 5.10 together with the rest of the key input
parameters for the controller used in the numerical models. Note that a linear gain scheduling is
assumed for the controller, hence the coefﬁcient of quadratic term in aerodynamic gain scheduling
K2 is set to zero. This means that the linear coefﬁcient in aerodynamic gain scheduling K1 is the
pitch angle where the aerodynamic torque gain has doubled from its value at zero pitch [38].

5.6
Model Veriﬁcation with Regular Waves
Following calibration of the two numerical models they are validated against regular waves in this
section. Regular waves represents an artiﬁcial sea state with a constant periodic nature. This allows
for an easy accessible analysis.
All four environmental conditions (EC) tested throughout the test campaign has been used to
validate the numerical models. This has been done in order to catch any possible differences in the
models depending on the EC. The parameters specifying each sea state; signiﬁcant wave height,
Hs, peak period, Tp, wind speed, Vhub as well as the operational parameters; blade pitch, θp and
rotational speed, Ω, is presented in Table 5.11. Since this section concerns regular waves, Hs is
taken as wave height and Tp as wave period.
The wind speeds listed in Table 5.11 deviates to the ones stated in Table 3.1 in Section 3.1 where
the environmental conditions are deﬁned. This is due to limitations of the wind generators. Thus,
the wind in the numerical models are also limited to the one of the experiments.
Four different simulations for each EC has been carried out in order to cover regular waves with
and without wind as well as misaligned waves with and without wind. Each simulation has a length
of 240 seconds.

motions in sway, roll and yaw were seen in the test results. These motions are considered to be
present due to imperfections in the experimental set-up, i.e. not perfect alignment of the ﬂoater to
the wave maker. In addition, mooring line tensions for the two downwind mooring lines and tower
top shear force is shown in Appendix I.1.

In the view of both mooring line tension and nacelle acceleration providing good agreements
between measured and simulated response. The measured pitch motion is deemed unreliable. One
of the reasons that the measured pitch is faulty is the accuracy of the Qualisys system as mentioned
when dealing with heave. The accuracy for rotational motions may affect the readings considering
the very small amplitude of pitch motion being within the accuracy measure, which is also very
limited for a TLP.
114
==PAGE:135==
In the interest of being able to compare the pitch response of the ﬂoater, a solution similar to the
one in [11] has been sought. Thus, a linear recalibration factor, γ, has been calculated by coupling
the in-plane response from surge, ξ1, and pitch,ξ5, as shown in (5.18). By averaging the beta factor
for all four sea states and the two numerical models a factor of γpitch = 0.22 was applied to the
measured pitch response yielding the result seen in Figure 5.17.
ξ5,num
ξ1,num
= γ ξ5,exp
ξ1,exp
(5.18)
It was subsequently found that due to similarity between pitch and roll motions the same method
had to be applied for the response in roll when running misaligned waves. In that case considering
sway and roll, a factor of γroll = 0.14 was computed and applied.

Comparing the pitch response after the γpitch factor is applied in Figure 5.17 reveals that the

spikes and poor readings as shown in Section 4.2.1. In addition, the numerical models does not
adequately capture the response from the second harmonic wave which is present in all four EC’s.
5.6.2
Response to Regular Waves with Wind
All four EC’s are run again with wind and can be seen in Appendix I.2. Here, Figure 5.18, EC5 is
presented as it represents rated wind speed and thereby the largest inﬂuence of the wind is seen
here, i.e. largest aerodynamic thrust. The turbine is operating with constant rotational speed and
blade pitch setting in a turbulent wind ﬂow matching the TI measured for the wind ﬁeld, see Section
4.1.1. For the operational turbine parameters see Table 5.11.
115
==PAGE:136==
Model Veriﬁcation with Regular Waves
Figure 5.18 shows all 6 degrees of freedom of the ﬂoater. The wind introduces a permanent offset
in surge corresponding to approximately 0.05 m, which the two numerical models capture. In
neither the experiments nor the numerical models an effect of aerodynamic damping is noticeable.
Furthermore, a set-down in heave is expected due to the permanent offset in surge. This is seen
by the numerical models, contrary to the measured response in heave, which shows close to no
change in mean value. Both the numerical and measured response are within the accuracy of the
Qualisys system. Thus, the heave response is taken with some uncertainty. Pitch response does also
include a mean offset due to the wind forcing. The measured response shows a larger amplitude
motion than the numerical models depict and in addition, a slightly higher mean value. Introducing
turbulent wind loading a degree of asymmetry will show in the response; seen in sway, roll and
yaw. This is present in all three cases. A small sway, roll and yaw response is also present without
wind. This is due to imperfect alignment of the ﬂoater in the basin.

The effect of wind on line tension and nacelle accelerations are shown in Figure 5.19. With a
