5.6.3
Response to Misaligned Regular Waves
During the test campaign, all waves were not run without the ﬂoater in the basin due to limited
time. Therefore, the misaligned waves used as input for the numerical models were measured by
WG8 in the actual test runs and then translated to the position of the ﬂoater through the frequency
domain. The method is explained in Section 5.3.3. The analysis between experimental and the two
numerical models are carried out in the global coordinate system contrary to the transformation
applied in Section 4.4, where the response is transformed into a coordinate system aligned with the
wave orthogonal. The latter is not chosen in order to evaluate the performance of the models in
predicting signiﬁcant out of plane motion, i.e. sway. The cases compared are with waves only. The
response in surge, sway and heave for two different EC’s are compared in Figure 5.20, where the

Taking a closer look at the yaw degree of freedom FAST estimates a larger response than HAWC2
across all EC’s, see Appendix I.3 for EC3 and EC6. It is possible that the reason for this difference
between the models originates from how the excitation loads are applied to the strucure is computed.
FAST computes the hydrodynamic loads in the ﬂoaters ﬁxed undisplaced position x, y, z = (0, 0, 0)
and subsequently applies the calculated loads to the actual position of the ﬂoater. The version of
the external system DLL ESYSWAMIT implemented in HAWC2 for this project also computes the
hydrodynamic loads in the ﬂoaters undisplaced position. Contrary to FAST, however, HAWC2
still allows for rotation of the ﬂoater, as seen in the source code for the ESYSWAMIT module
ESYSWamit.f90. Rotation of the ﬂoater causes a difference in the hydrodynamic forces read
from WAMIT, and thereby a possible smaller response. To support this reasoning, the added mass,
118
==PAGE:139==
damping and excitation forces are shown in Appendix H.
The acceleration in x- and y-direction is shown in Figure 5.22. The numerical models capture the
nacelle fore-aft acceleration (x-direction) while not inﬂuenced signiﬁcantly by the introduction of
misaligned waves. Seeing that the sway motions are over- and underestimated by the numerical
models in the two EC’s respectively, it is no surprise that the side-side accelerations behave the
same due to coupling. In side-side accelerations, forcing originating from double the incident wave
frequency is present in both EC’s.

CHAPTER 6
Numerical Results with Irregular
Waves
This chapter presents selected results with irregular waves where the numerical models are compared
to experimental results as well as to each other. The numerical models has been tuned and validated
as presented in the previous chapter. The irregular sea states are based on Pierson-Moskowitz
spectrum and the measured wave elevation has been used as input for the two models.
6.1
Method of Analysis - Probabilistic Method
In the analysis of loads, accelerations and displacements by irregular waves a probabilistic method
was applied. This method is again chosen here in the numerical section since it is undesirable to
look at time series which is governed by random processes such as irregular wind and turbulent
wind. The method applied is the same as described in Section 4.4. Yet, a quick recap is given
here. Independence of each peak is secured through a down crossing analysis of the wave elevation,
which deﬁnes the window in which the largest peak is found. Subsequently, the peaks are sorted
from minimum to maximum and assigned an index, i, out of a total number of peaks N. Finally,
the probability of exceedance of an arbitrary peak is calculated by (4.4),
P = 1 −
i
N + 1
(6.1)
This way the largest peak will correspond to the lowest probability and vice versa.
6.2
Response to Irregular Waves
As an introduction to irregular waves, surge, heave, pitch, accelerations and mooring line tension
will be presented here. Since no response should be present in sway, roll and yaw, these are not
presented her, but are available in Appendix J.
121
==PAGE:142==

numerical models to match surge motions in irregular waves is not convincing. The best agreement
is seen for EC8 where the numerical models agree with the experiments up to the 85 % quantile. In
general, FAST predicts slightly larger motions.

Mooring Lines and Accelerations
In Figure 6.4 the exceedance probability of the front mooring line is shown for all four sea states.
The two downwind mooring lines are shown in Appendix J.1. The mooring lines are normalized by
their initial tension, τ0, measured in equilibrium position. As the waves are growing, so are the
highest tensions in the mooring lines. This tendency is also captured in the two numerical models,
which produces very similar predictions (between FAST and HAWC2) of the tension. Compared
with measured response, the agreement between models and measured response increases as well.
The same picture was seen for pitch motions and since they are closely related, increased pitch will
cause increased tension in the front spoke, it may imply that the mooring line stiffness is too high.
The fore-aft nacelle accelerations are generally under estimated by the numerical models as seen
in Figure 6.5. Yet, the predictions from FAST and HAWC2 agrees very well. It is likely that the
difference to the measured response is linked to the smaller pitch motions seen in Figure 6.3.

Improved Model
6.3
Improved Model
Based on the deviations seen in pitch and heave it seems that the models are too stiff in these
degrees of freedom. By lowering the mooring line stiffness, EAmoor, by 10 % the pitch and
heave motions are expected to increase and thereby get closer to the experimental results. This
is acceptable, since some uncertainty is connected with the experimental determination of the
mooring line stiffness, see Section 5.1.1. Figure 6.6 shows how surge, heave, pitch, fore-aft nacelle
acceleration and front mooring line tension reacts to the decreased mooring line stiffness. The
black markers represent(×) FAST, and (△) HAWC2 in the following ﬁgures. No change in surge
is seen, since surge motion is dominated by hydrodynamic forces. Looking at heave and pitch, a
much better ﬁt to the experiments is seen. Nevertheless, the change in mooring line stiffness did not
affect fore-aft nacelle acceleration or mooring line tension noticeable towards a better ﬁt. The same
tendencies are seen for EC5, which is included in Appendix, Figure J.3. But, for this sea state the
numerical models seem to over estimate small heave motions while the increase in pitch motion is
inadequate. Thus, in order to optimize, heave, pitch and surge motion more substantial changes and
insight is needed, which was not possible to pursue within the time limit of this project. Therefore,
the 10 % decrease in mooring line stiffness is not incorporated in the simulations presented in the
rest of this chapter.

6.4
Pure Linear Response
The surge response calculated by FAST and HAWC2 in the previous section showed a response
which deviates from the measured response signiﬁcantly when considering larger motions which
is also shown in Figure 6.7b for aligned irregular waves in EC6. The reason for this deviation
was investigated, and it was found that subsequent to a steep wave the structure experiences an
increased short term offset in mean displacement as seen in Figure 6.7(b) at time t ≈463 s.
This behaviour is to be considered as non-linear behaviour. Therefore, the method described
in Section 4.6 was applied to the experimental reults for the irregular surge signal in order to
separate ﬁrst and third order forcing from second and fourth order forcing. The ﬁrst (odd) order
response is seen as the black circles and dotted line in Figure 6.7 (a) and (b) respectively. Looking
at Power Spectral Density plot and the time series, disregarding the small phase difference, it
becomes clear that the mean offset in the surge response is due to response at the surge natural
frequency caused by sub-harmonic forcing from the waves which cannot be captured in the nu-
merical models which are based on linear wave kinematics and linear hydrodynamic forcing models.

Figure 6.7: Analysis of pure linear response in surge for EC6 irregular waves. (a) Exceedance
probability plot showing both original and linearised responses for experiment and numerical
models. b) Zoom of surge timeseries and PSD of surge for comparison of the linearised numerical
model and originally run numerical models.
In order to compare the linearised experimental surge response consistently, the two numerical
models has been run with a linearised wave elevation signal. The wave signal has been linearised
by ﬁltering in the frequency domain, disregarding all frequencies not located around the main wave
frequency range, i.e. not including sub and higher harmonics. The outcome of running FAST
and HAWC2 with a linearised wave signal is shown in Figure 6.7(a) as black (×) FAST, and (△)
HAWC2 where the numerical runs based on the pure measured wave elevation is kept for reference
in plot 6.7(b). By running the numerical models with the linearised wave signal they approach
127
==PAGE:148==
Effect of Wind
the linearised measurements only deviating noticeable for the largest 1 % of the surge motions.
This implies that signiﬁcant non-linear behaviour in surge degree of freedom is present for the
KIER-TLP tested in this project. A behaviour which was not found in a previous numerical study
of model tests with a TLP [14]. The biggest change between the TLP investigated in [14] and
the KIER-TILP are the size and volume of the spokes which for the KIER-TLP is considerable.
Nonetheless, more work has to be put into explaining why the non-linear plays a signiﬁcant role
for the KIER-TLP. Plots similar to Figre 6.7b for the three other sea states are found in Appendix
J.3 as well as exceedance plots in style with Figure 6.7a.
6.5
Effect of Wind
The four EC’s were run with wind and constant RPM and constant pitch settings as prescribed
in Table 5.11. Results from all EC 3,6 and 8 are available in Appendix J.4, while the analysis
in this section will focus on EC5 (rated wind speed) in line with the analysis of wind in regular
waves. The response in 6DOF motion has been averaged, in order to see the effect of wind in direct
comparison to the no wind case. Thus, a mean offset in surge, roll and pitch has been removed. The
non-averaged ﬁgures are available in Appendix J.5. The averaged six degrees of freedom response
for EC5 is shown in Figure 6.8. Here the green markers depicts the results without wind likewise
for EC5 in order to give a frame of reference.
Surge, heave and pitch
The numerical models predict the surge motion very well up to the 99 % quantile with FAST
showing a little higher motions. Though, they seem to increase slightly contrary to decrease as is
seen from the experiments. Looking at the heave motion it is seen that heave increases with wind.
The numerical models increase more proportionally to the measured signal and provides a better
ﬁt than without wind. Please keep in mind that the measured heave motion is linked to a certain
degree of uncertainty. For pitch, the measured response is affected by aerodynamic damping as
also stated in previous chapter. The numerical models seem affected to smaller degree. In between
the numerical models, FAST predicts slightly higher motion.
Sway, roll, yaw
The introduction of turbulent wind introduces some asymmetry in the model. The asymmetry
is caused by the changing thrust force due to changing azimuth position and the other platform
motions which leads to response in yaw and secondary sway and roll. Measured sway motions are
seen to be more or less unaffected by the introduction of wind and so are roll motions which is only
slightly larger for the wind case. In spite of this, the numerical models do predict some limited
sway and roll motions when they are exposed to wind as expected. Furthermore, yaw motions are
signiﬁcantly increased. Considering the out of plane motions HAWC2 predicts a larger response
compared with FAST.

numerical models is not seen to increase with the inclusion of wind. Looking at the fore-aft nacelle
accelerations, anacx, the numerical models predict more or less the same as for the case without
wind. Side-side accelerations are now present in the numerical models which corresponds well
with the presence of sway and roll motions.

CHAPTER 7
Model Controller Comparison
This chapter will study the inclusion of control mechanisms in the numerical simulations and
compare with experiments. The Basic DTU Wind Energy Controller has been implemented in the
two numerical models as described in Section 2.5.1 where the main parameters for the controller
is listed in Table 5.10. First, the controller performance is tested in a case where only wind is
present, next with the inclusion of regular waves and lastly with irregular waves. Emphasis will be
put on the comparison of FAST and HAWC2, since the wind loading is generated in HAWC2 and
the controller is not the exact same as in the experiments. The analysis presented will only focus
of a few in plane motions and parameters. Due to unstable performance of the controller in the
transition region from partial to full load, EC5 could not be the subject of the controller comparison.
It was tried to solve the issue, yet it was not ﬁxed before the end of this project. Therefore, EC8
has been chosen as the subject in this analysis of the comparison of controller performance.
7.1
Control in Only Wind
As a part of the test matrix, Figure 3.3, wind only test were performed with the active controller
for three wind speeds 1.1 m/s, 1.5 m/s and 2.2 m/s. These tests are used as a baseline for the
controller performance in the two numerical models. The highest wind speed is presented here
in Figure 7.1 where the controller parameters; blade pitch θp, Rotational speed Ω, and generator
torque Qg are presented together with surge and pitch motion as well as nacelle acceleration and
normalized front mooring line tension τ3/τ0. The wind measurement from the experiments is only
included to give a frame of reference since it was measured right in front of the wind generator
(0.1 m/s is subtracted as a rule of thumb achieved during the test campaign). It therefore do not
include the turbulent behaviour which will be generated as the ﬂow mixes with the surrounding
air. The wind time series for the numerical models are produced in HAWC2 and imported into
FAST. All six degrees of freedom of the ﬂoater is included in Appendix K, Figure K.1. Figure
7.1 presents 100 seconds of the wind only simulation where control is active. It can be seen that
blade pitch and rotational speed is coherent with the test. The torque reading cannot be expected to
match. This is due to the large amount of friction in the drive train on the physical turbine model.
Thus, the plot serves as a comparison of the predictions from the two numerical models. Looking
133
==PAGE:154==
Control and Regular Waves
at the surge motion, FAST reveals signiﬁcant oscillations with a frequency matching the surge
natural frequency. HAWC2 shows a response very close to the experiments. The measured pitch,
nacelle acceleration and mooring line tension show oscillations at 1P and 3P frequencies. This
is not showing in the numerical models. The numerical models predict a smaller mean offset in
pitch which also affects the mooring line tension. The amplitude of the nacelle acceleration are
signiﬁcantly larger than in the numerical models, which is the result of 1P and 3P forcing. Event
though the controller performance is not optimum, it was decided to proceed with it in its current
state.

7.2
Control and Regular Waves
With the wind only performance of the controller accepted, the performance is now tested in
regular waves corresponding to the highest sea state, EC8. A ten second window showing the same
operational parameters, motions, force and acceleration, as was the case for no wind, is shown
in Figure 7.2. Likewise, for a ﬁgure with all six degrees of freedom showing the same window
and including a PSD plot see Appendix K, Figure K.2. The inclusion of regular waves causes
the differences in surge, pitch, nacelle acceleration and mooring line tension, that were present in
Figure 7.1, to diminish. Hence, the waves seem to govern the response although a small offset is
seen in the surge signal between the numerical models and the measured response. The rotation
134
==PAGE:155==
speed of the rotor is matched perfectly by the numerical models, subject to the changes in the wave
elevation. Difference between the two numerical models now show even clearer than in the case
without wind when looking at blade pitch and torque. Looking at blade pitch motions, the average
is a bit higher for the numerical models than in the experiments. More interesting, however, is
it that the two numerical models do not adjust the blade pitch in the same manner even though
the controller is the same. This may be linked with the large variation in torque seen for HAWC2
while FAST only shows small variations in torque. Yet, the models predict the same mean torque.
With these results in mind, the controller is capable of reproducing the in-line motions despite the
controller behaviour is not exactly the same as in the experiment.

hydrodynamic forcing, see Section 6.4. In order to give better insight on the exceedance plots

Figure 7.3: Performance of implemented controller in highest wind 2.2 m/s and irregular waves,
EC8, compared to experiments.
To evaluate the performance of the numerical models when active control is implemented, the
numerical and experimental results from a test run where the turbine was operating at the same
wind speed but with a ﬁxed rotational speed and ﬁxed blade pitch according to Table 5.11. This
provide insight on how the controller affects the responses relative to a ﬁxed case. The "ﬁxed"
case is shown in green and has the same markers, specifying whether it is experimental (o), or the
two numerical models FAST (x) and HAWC2 (△). Looking at the in-line forces, surge, heave and
pitch a response very similar to the ﬁxed case is seen. The two numerical models shows a good
and very similar ﬁt in surge up to the 90 % quantile, hereafter HAWC2 and FAST starts to deviate
with FAST predicting the highest motion. The two numerical models agrees well in heave and
predicts nearly the same motions as was present in the ﬁxed case. Considering pitch, however, a
large deviation is seen between FAST and HAWC2. They predict the same pitch motion up to
the 80 % quantile after which HAWC2 predicts higher and higher loads exceeding the measured
response. Looking at the time series reveals that no strange behaviour is seen besides from the
largest peaks being over estimated. FAST lies close the the measured response, however under
estimates slightly. The experimental measurements of the out-of-plane motions sway and roll
and yaw are as mentioned earlier inﬂuenced by the the ﬂoater being positioned in the basin with
136
==PAGE:157==
a small yaw error. therefore it is the difference between the cases of active control and ﬁxed
operation that is interesting. Sway motions seem unaffected by the implementation of control, thus
control do not affect sway noticeable. Looking at roll, the experiments experienced slightly larger
motions. HAWC2, on the other hand, depicts lower roll motions due to the active controller. This
is different from FAST which does not predict much difference in roll compared to the case with
ﬁxed operation. Yaw motions is seemed to increase to some extent in the numerical models due to
inclusion of active control and thereby changing blade pitch.

CHAPTER 8
Numerical Results with Focused
waves
Focused waves are extreme events that is artiﬁcially created by superposition of waves with different
frequencies to create constructive interference at a speciﬁed time and place as explained in Section
4.5 where the focused waves from the physical model tests are studied. The focused wave is
interesting to compare with numerical models since its nature of impulsive load will result in a
transient response of the structure. Simulations have been run for all four sea states with wind,
ﬁxed turbine operation, and without wind. Presented in this chapter is EC8 since this is the largest
sea state, and EC5 when considering wind since it corresponds to rated wind speed. All cases are
shown in Appendix L. The analysis presented here will focus on the numerical models capability
to reproduce the measured signals rather than why the signals look the way they do. The latter is
covered in Section 4.5.
8.1
Focused Wave Without Wind
In the following analysis only in-plane motions, mooring line tension in the front and downwind
mooring line and fore-aft nacelle acceleration are presented. The left column of plots will show
time series and the right column display plots of the PSD function.
Focused Wave EC8
In Figure 8.1 the wave elevation is shown together with the in-line motions. Surge motion is
captured well regarding the phase of the forcing. But, as seen in the analysis of irregular waves
the numerical models are based on linear forcing models which do not capture the non-linear
behaviour resulting in an offset in mean surge displacement. With a view to the PSD plot, the
numerical models capture well the energy content in the forcing originating from the wave forcing
spectra. The energy content at the surge natural frequency is underestimated, however, this is
heavily inﬂuenced by the mean offset. It is seen that the ﬁrst trough in heave motion is captured.
Nonetheless, the rest of the response deviates from the measured signal. The pitch motion is
reproduced well by the numerical models, yet when looking at the PSD plot the same tendencies as
139
==PAGE:160==
Focused Wave Without Wind
in the surge motion is seen; the forcing at the wave frequencies are captured well while the energy
content at the surge natural frequency is not captured. HAWC2 on the other hand shows vibrations
at 2 Hz, which may be dedicated to the mooring-induced pitching motion.

Figure 8.1: Response of in-line degrees of freedom for focused wave, no wind, EC8
The in-line nacelle acceleration together with front and downwind mooring line tension is shown in
8.2. The amplitude of nacelle acceleration is captured excellently, with the footnote that HAWC2
predicts oscillations at 2 Hz as also seen for pitch. Generally, the mooring line predictions are good
with the maximum tension in the two cables being τ1 ≈τ3 = 1.7 τ0 and the minimum tension
being τ1 ≈τ3 = 0.34 τ0 according to the numerical models. The tests resulted in the downwind
mooring line showing higher tension. This is odd, since previous test campaigns [9] showed an
increase in the front mooring line. An increase which is not captured by the numerical models
either. The numerical models predict a bit of motion at the heave natural frequency of 6 Hz in both
mooring lines and especially for HAWC2 a small amount of energy at 2 Hz. Overall, FAST and
HAWC2 shows up to be nearly in complete agreement when predicting in-line motions, mooring
tensions and nacelle acceleration in focused waves.

Focused Wave EC5
The focused wave for EC5 without wind is brieﬂy presented here to give a reference for the accuracy
of the numerical models before introducing wind for this sea state. Figure 8.3 shows the of in-line
degrees of freedom response for EC5 focused wave with no wind. The surge motion is matched
nicely for the ﬁrst two peaks before a mean offset shows in the test followed by a free decay in
surge. The "free decay" taking place is not captured by the numerical models. In heave, a constant
oscillation of 6 Hz (heave natural frequency) is seen, this is also seen for EC3, see Appendix Figure
L.1, but not for EC6 and EC8, the reason for this is not straight forward and further analysis has be
to done. Considering pitch, the numerical models somewhat underestimate the motion, which is
also seen in the PSD plot where the numerical models show less energy at the wave frequencies.
Again, it is evident that some vibrations occur at 2 Hz after the wave have hit in the HAWC2 model.
Looking at Figure 8.4 the 2 Hz vibrations are also signiﬁcant in the nacelle acceleration estimated
by HAWC2. Furthermore, the heave vibrations are to a high degree visible in the mooring lines as
well. The mooring line tensions are slightly under estimated, visible in both time and frequency
domain. In addition, the 2 Hz vibrations also play a role for the front mooring tension τ3.

8.2
Focused Wave with Wind EC5
In this section a focused wave with wind is shown for EC5 which corresponds to rated wind speed.
When conducting the tests, the wind was applied on the turbine with ﬁxed operational parameters
until the turbine rested in its new steady-state position. Subsequently, the waves were started and
data recorded. The same is done for the numerical models where wind is applied for 30 s. before
the wave signal starts. This transient region is not included in the analysis. The inﬂuence of wind
for all four sea states can be found in Appendix L.1.
Based on the results from Section 4.3 and looking at regular waves with wind, the inclusion of wind
will induce an offset in surge, heave, pitch and line tension. Apart from the offset, the wind does not
inﬂuence the goodness of ﬁt for the numerical models as seen in Figure 8.5. The numerical models
now shows energy at the surge natural frequency and in time series is seen to catch the damped
decay in surge. The numerical models predict a larger offset in heave than what is measured in the
tests. Nonetheless, the amplitude of the response is similar to the experiment. The same picture is
seen for pitch.

==PAGE:165==
CHAPTER 9
Conclusions
In this thesis, the dynamic response of a ﬂoating wind turbine was investigated by experimental
testing and was modelled in FAST and HAWC2. The scaled model consisted of a TLP model
designed by the Korea Institute of Energy Research and the scaled 1/60 DTU 10 MW reference
wind turbine. This thesis contains both an experimental and a numerical study and a comparison
between them. The investigation included the response in regular waves, irregular waves, focused
waves and a low turbulent wind ﬁeld tested with different turbine controllers. The experiments
were carried out in the wave basin at Danish Hydraulic Institute (DHI), where a wind generator had
been mounted to produce the wind climate. The experimental results have played an important role
in the validation of the two numerical models, the aero-hydro-servo-elastic codes; HAWC2 and
FAST.
The wind ﬁeld produced by the wind generators was reasonably uniform with a maximum mean
wind velocity of ≈2.2 m/s at hub height. For the environmental conditions that required higher
mean wind speeds, it was decided to operate the wind turbine at this wind speed. The turbulence
intensity of the wind ﬁeld was 3.5%.
The waves were generated by a wave maker capable of generating misaligned and spread waves.
The regular waves showed a maximum difference of 9% for wave height and 0.6% in wave period.
For the irregular sea states the maximum difference was 4.3% for the signiﬁcant wave height. A
maximum difference of 17% was found for the peak period in EC8 which was due to a ﬂat peak in
the measured wave spectrum. In addition, the focused waves were analysed and showed a very
good math with theory.
Before the wave tests the aerodynamic performance of the ﬁxed model scaled turbine was suc-
cessfully tuned to match the downscaled steady thrust curve of the 10 MW reference turbine. The
turbine was then mounted on the ﬂoater and free decay tests in still water provided insight of the
structural natural frequencies and damping ratios.
From the results with regular and irregular waves damping could be observed in surge and was even
more pronounced in pitch when wind was included. Generally in aligned waves minor responses
145
==PAGE:166==
CHAPTER 9. CONCLUSIONS
were seen in out-of-plane motions and the overall trend was an increase in response as the waves
grew. This implies an imperfect alignment of the ﬂoater in the basin and is very difﬁcult to avoid.
This test campaign also involved the investigation of three different controllers; a controller tuned
for offshore, a controller tuned for onshore and an open-loop controller as reference. The sensitivity
of the torque reading for the onshore controller resulted in high oscillations in blade pitch, yielding
an increased response in surge, pitch and yaw for all wave types. It was also observed that the pitch
motion governed the mooring line tensions, thus the onshore controller yielded the highest tensions
in the front mooring line and lowest in the downwind lines. The shut down cases of the offshore
controller led to larger surge displacement when the shut down was initialised right before the wave
impact.
The experiments showed that in spread waves the pitch and surge motion were reduced, while the
out-of-plane motions were increased remarkably, especially the yaw response.
In the numerical part a panel model of the ﬂoater was successfully run in WAMIT to predict the
radiation and diffraction forces which was imported to HAWC2 and FAST. Nonlinear viscous
damping effects was implemented separately through strip theory in both codes. The numerical
models were ﬁrst adapted to the new ﬂoater concept and the changes to the tower, before calibrated
through comparison of key parameters acquired in the tests, such as tower decay, steady thrust
curve and free decay tests in water. The models were validated by comparing the dynamic response
in regular waves with the experimental results both with and without wind. Also the case with
wave misalignment was used to validate the out-of-plane response of the models. Once validated,
the numerical models were used to simulate the response to irregular waves. This would include
cases with and without wind, followed by investigating the effect of misalignment. An analysis of
the effect of the model controller in both numerical models were conducted and ﬁnally, a set of
extreme focused events were simulated with and without wind.
An overall good agreement between the simulation results from FAST and HAWC2 was found,
despite some deviations for the largest motions. The comparison between the two models and
measurements showed an acceptable agreement for in-line motions in the most severe cases.
However for the lower environmental conditions, both models under predicted the response in
irregular waves and possible reasons for this has been investigated. It appeared that the TLP ﬂoater
design introduced signiﬁcant non-linear behaviour in surge response. Furthermore the transverse
motions were not possible to match due to asymmetry in the alignment of the ﬂoater in the basin.
The implemented model controller in FAST and HAWC2 was able to capture some of the same
tendencies as the offshore controller used in the experiments. Lastly the numerical models proved
ﬁt to capture the transient dynamics of extreme focused waves.
For future investigations it is recommended to:
• extend the numerical analysis to take several wind turbulence seeds into account
• improve the resolution of the motion capture system and execution of free decay tests
• implement second order pre-computed wave kinematics to numerical models
146
==PAGE:167==
• improve the numerical model controller such that it mimics the controller used in experiments
better
In conclusion, the present work has contributed to a successful validation of the TLP ﬂoater design
by KIER and has shown the importance of conducting lab scale experiments. These tests play an
important role in validating the numerical models of ﬂoating wind turbines, adding both reliability
and conﬁdence to the ﬁeld of research, which will help push the offshore wind industry forward.
147

APPENDIX B
Loads on Offshore Wind Turbines
B.1
Aerodynamic loading
The Unsteady BEM Model
At least six coordinate systems are necessary in the unsteady BEM model, as seen in Figure 2.3,
since the aerodynamic loads depends on the relative wind seen by the blades where the wind ﬁeld
is referred to the ground.
Coordinate system 1 corresponds to the inertial coordinate system, located at the sea bottom and
all the other systems are moving along with the ﬂoater. System 2 is placed at ﬂoater platform and
is where the hydrodynamic forces are derived, whereas system 3 is located at the transition point
between ﬂoater top and tower bottom. The initial system and the ﬂoater coordinate system are
related with a transformation matrix, which takes into account platform rotations, i.e. rotations
about the x-axis (roll, ξ4), the y-axis (pitch, ξ5) and the z-axis (yaw, ξ6):
a1x =

model.

constructed by S. Øye, is a dynamic ﬁlter for the induced velocities consisting of two ﬁrst order
differential equations:

Dynamic stall
The wind seen from a point on the blade changes constantly due to wind shear, tower interference,
yaw misalignment and turbulence. The change in the wind has a directly impact on the angle of
attack and the effect of changing the angle of attack of the blade will not appear instantaneously
on the loads but will take place with a time delay. This is why a dynamic stall model must be
implemented.
Stall effects occur when leaving the linear lift region and it is a phenomenon that affects airfoils,
wings, towers and rotors in unsteady ﬂows [18]. It is due to rapidly change in the inﬂow conditions
and/or the angle of attack. These pitching oscillations can cause a leading edge vortex to be shed
creating additional suction over the upper surface of the airfoil as it travels downstream. This
results in an brieﬂy increased lift and stall delay, but as soon as the vortex detach the trailing edge
the lift of the blade reduces dramatically and a sudden increase in the pitching moment occurs.
This is where full stall takes place. When the angle of attack decreases and falls below the static
stall value again, the ﬂow re-attaches from the leading to the trailing edge again.
The dynamic stall model implemented to the unsteady BEM model is conducted by S. Øye. This
model only takes the trailing edge stall into account and it is only represented with one separation
function, fs, which makes it very simple to use. The degree of stall is described by fs as:
Cl = fsCl,inv(α) + (1 −fs)Cl,fs(α)
(B.21)
Where Cl,inv is the lift coefﬁcient for inviscid ﬂow without any separation and Cl,fs is the lift
coefﬁcient for fully separated ﬂow. The values of these two coefﬁcients Cl,inv and Cl,fs together
with fst
s which is the value of the separation function fs that reproduces the static airfoil data when
applied in equation (B.21), are looked up for a speciﬁc angle of attack α in the airfoil dataﬁles. The
VII
==PAGE:180==
Aerodynamic loading
assumption of the model is that the separation function fs will always try to get back to the static
value:
fs(t + ∆t) = fst
s + (fs(t) −fst

(B.22)
With τ being a time constant depending on the chord and the relative velocity seen by the blade
and can be approximated to be equal to 4c/Vrel. The dynamic stall model replaces the old static
lift coefﬁcient table look-up method with an improved dynamic lift coefﬁcient estimation which
allows for a time delay.
Wind shear model
In order to reproduce a more realistic wind proﬁle, thus also more realistic loads on the wind
turbine, than an uniform inﬂow, the boundary layer caused by thermal changes in height, hills, tress,
buildings etc. must be included as seen in Figure B.2. This is called the atmospheric boundary
layer. At neutral atmospheric stability conditions, one wind shear model recommended by IEC is
the power law proﬁle modelled by:
V0(x) = V0(H)

ν

where H denotes hub height, x the distance from the surface and ν is controlling the amount
of shear and is typically around 0.1 and 0.25. It should be noted, that the turbulent wind of the
atmospheric part of the wind is not included in the simple equation (B.23).
Figure B.2: Variation of the wind ﬁeld due to wind shear [18]
Turbulence
In addition to the steady and periodic aerodynamic forces, the wind turbine is also exposed to
randomly and rapidly ﬂuctuating aerodynamic forces induced by gusts, turbulence and dynamic
VIII
==PAGE:181==
effects. Turbulence varies in intensity and is characterized as a random variation of wind speed in
time and space around a mean value:
TI =
σ
V 10min
(B.24)
The resulting ﬂuctuating loads on the wind turbine blades are generally the main cause of fatigue
and determine the lifetime estimate of a wind turbine. Extreme aerodynamic loads are usually
dominated by gust, which is a sudden increase in wind speed over short duration.
Time series of the turbulent wind can be constructed from a known Power Spectral Density (PSD)
function using an inverse discrete Fourier transform. Such a time series at one single point in space
can be computed using one of the PSD functions that approximate the atmospheric boundary layer,
such as the Von Karman spectrum described by Mann (2012) [55]:

Tower shadow

is reﬂected as a drop in the rotor thrust with a frequency of three times the rotor frequency (3P).

Linear Wave Theory
The theory of linear waves is the foundation for the hydrodynamic loads affecting the ﬂoater. This
section will give a brief introduction to the linearised wave problem often denoted as Airy Theory
or Stokes 1st order. Linear wave theory is valid when the waves have a small amplitude compared
XI
==PAGE:184==
Linear Wave Theory
to their wavelength, H/λ < 0.05. Since the theory is based on potential ﬂow, it is required that the
ﬂow is incompressible, inviscid and irrotational. Furthermore, both the water depth and the period
is assumed constant. A schematic drawing of a monochromatic wave is shown in Figure B.5.
Figure B.5: Schematic drawing of regular wave in 2D [20]
Regular waves

problem untouched.

Irregular Waves

tion is used to create a wave elevation consisting of a sum of statistically independent waves with
different periods and amplitudes and random phases. The irregular waves are in the sea states used
in this project created by the Pierson-Moskowitz spectrum,

APPENDIX C
WAMIT Theory
WAMIT is a radiation/diffraction program for analysing the interaction of plane progressive waves
with one or multiple interacting offshore structures in ﬁnite or inﬁnite depth. The structure may be
located at any position between the bottom and the free surface. WAMIT allows for freely ﬂoating,
restrained or ﬁxed bodies and is based on a three-dimensional panel method. WAMIT utilises a
cartesian coordinate system (x, y, z) as illustrated in Figure C.1. Wave heading angles are deﬁned
by β relative to the x-axis. Figure C.1 also shows the possibility of deﬁning a separate set of body
coordinates. The WAMIT coordinate system is identical to the one used in FAST. The coordinate
system applied in HAWC2, however, is deﬁned in 5.3.1.
XV
==PAGE:188==
APPENDIX C. WAMIT THEORY
Figure C.1: Sketch deﬁning the global (X,Y,Z) and local (x,y,z) coordinatesystem. β represents
wave heading
WAMIT is based on a potential ﬂow formulation of the ﬂow ﬁeld where the free-surface and
body-boundary conditions are linearised. The potential ﬂow formulation assumes that the ﬂow is
incompressible, inviscid and irrotational. In this description of the theory, the starting point will be
to deﬁne the velocity vector u:
u =


u
v
w

,
(C.1)
where u, v, w are the velocity components in the x, y, z direction respectively. The ﬂow can be
assumed incompressible as long as the ﬂuid particle velocity is small relative to the speed of sound
in the ﬂuid, which is the case for most applications of surface waves. Viscous forces are only
signiﬁcant in boundary layers. Boundary layers considered when dealing with marine structures
are generally very thin due to relatively high Reynolds numbers. Thus for the bulk of the ﬂuid,
viscosity is neglected and is therefore considered inviscid [21, p.6]. Irrotationality, or circulation,
of the ﬂuid is ensured by application of Kelvin’s theorem of the conservation of circulation. It
states that for an ideal ﬂuid acted upon by conservative forces, the circulation is constant seeing
that no shear stresses acts in an inviscid ﬂuid. Thus, if the ﬂuid is initially at rest, it will remain
irrotational [21, p. 103]. Mathematically, the circulation of a ﬂuid is described by the curl of the
XVI
==PAGE:189==
velocity and should be equal to zero for an irrotational ﬂuid,
∇× u =


∂u
∂y −∂v
∂x
∂v
∂z −∂w
∂y
∂w
∂x −∂u
∂z


= 0
(C.2)
When the ﬂow is irrotational, the velocity ﬁeld can be represented as the gradient of a scalar
function, the velocity potential Φ, of three coordinates and time t,
u = ∇Φ =


∂Φ
∂x


(C.3)
this is shown in [21, p. 106].
For an ideal ﬂuid, the continuity equation must be satisﬁed. This is expressed as zero divergence of
the ﬂow ﬁeld,
∇· u = ∂u
∂x + ∂v
∂y + ∂w
∂z = 0
(C.4)
Insertion of the velocity potential (C.3) in the continuity equation (C.4) yields the Laplace equation
∇2Φ = ∂2u
∂x2 + ∂2v
∂y2 + ∂2w
∂z2 = 0
(C.5)
which expresses conservation of mass in terms of the velocity potential. A time harmonic solution,Φ,
to the potential is assumed,
Φ = ℜ(φeiωt),
(C.6)
where Re denotes the real part of the complex potential φ, ω is the frequency of the incident wave
and t is time. The complex potential φ satisﬁes boundaries concerning the free surface and the
bottom. The linearized kinematic boundary condition,
∂φ
∂z = 0
,
z = −h
ﬁnite depth
φ →0
,
z →−∞
inﬁnite depth
(C.7)
stating that the vertical velocity of water particles is zero, i.e. the bottom is impermeable. In
the case of deep water (inﬁnite water depth), the ﬂow velocities will go towards zero for z going
towards negative inﬁnity. The free surface condition is here presented as one containing both the
kinematic- and dynamic surface boundary condition,
∂φ
∂z −ω2
g φ = 0
,
z = 0
(C.8)
The kinematic surface boundary condition ensures that the vertical velocity of a particle at the
free surface is the same as the vertical velocity of the water surface while the dynamic boundary
XVII
==PAGE:190==
APPENDIX C. WAMIT THEORY
condition ensures that the pressure at the free surface is equal to the surrounding pressure. By
applying the boundary conditions to the complex potential, the velocity potential of a free incident
wave takes the form,
φ0 = igA
ω
cosh k(z + h)
cosh kh
e−ikx cos β−iky sin β
(C.9)
where β is the angle between the wave orthogonal and the positive x-axis as shown in Figure C.1.
The linear dispersion relation k that provides a connection between angular frequency, ω, and
the water depth, h, is deﬁned below. The dispersion relation and the coefﬁcients for the velocity
potential is obtained by satisfying the free surface boundary condition at mean water level (MWL),
ω2 = gk tanh kh
(C.10)
Due to the linearisation of the velocity potential, the principle of superposition applies. This makes
it possible to divide the potential into a radiation and a diffraction component respectively,
φ = φR + φD.
(C.11)
The radiated velocity potential, φR can be divided further into six potentials, φj , j = 1, 2, . . . , 6 as
seen in C.12, each corresponding the wave ﬁeld caused by a rigid body motion with unit amplitude
in the j’th degree of freedom [21, ch.6.15]
φR = iω
6
X
j=1
ξjφj
(C.12)
Here, ξj is the complex amplitude of the oscillatory motion of the rigid body in its six degrees of
freedom. In addition to (C.7) and (C.8) the radiation velocity potentials must also satisfy kinematic
boundary conditions associated with the body surface, which takes the form [21, ch. 6.15],
∂φj
∂n = iωnj,
∂φj
∂n = iω(r × n)j−3,
j = 1, 2, 3,
j = 4, 5, 6,
(C.13)
where n = (n1, n2, n3) is the unit normal vector on the body surface pointing into the body, i.e out
of the ﬂuid domain. r = (x, y, z) is the positional vector deﬁning the position of the body. Thus
C.13 relates the velocity of the ﬂuid particle with the velocity on a given body surface.
Contrary, the diffraction potential is independent of the body motions (ﬁxed body) and consists of
the incident and scattered wave potential,
φD = φ0 + φS
(C.14)
The scattering effect is present when the presence of a body disturbs the incident wave ﬁeld [21, ch.
6.15]. Since the body is ﬁxed in space, the normal derivative of the diffracted potential, ∂φA
∂n , must
vanish on the boundary surface SB,
∂φA
∂n = 0
on SB
(C.15)
XVIII
==PAGE:191==
In addition, the scattered velocity potential must satisfy the following boundary condition on the
body surface,
∂φS
∂n = −∂φ0
∂n
on SB
(C.16)
To ensure a unique solution to the boundary problem speciﬁed above, the radiated and scattered
velocity potential must satisfy the radiation condition which in three dimensions is formulated as
[21, ch.6.15],
φ ∝
1
(x2 + y2)
1
4
e−ik(x2+y2)
1
2 ,
as R →∞
(C.17)
where k is the wave number. This condition ensures that all waves, other than the incident wave
ﬁeld, must propagate away from the body an in the limit take the form (C.17).
WAMIT solves the described wave-body boundary value problem by applying Green’s theorem
with a source-only formulation in order to derive integral equations for the radiation and diffraction
velocity potentials on the body boundary [36]. In this way, the ﬂow is modelled by inserting sources
over the body surface Sb and applying Green’s Theorem. Below, C.18 and C.19 show the integral
equation for the radiation, φj and diffraction potential, φD, as presented in [36, ch. 15],
2πφj(x) +
ZZ
Sb
φj(ξ)∂G(ξ; x)
∂nξ
dξ =
ZZ
Sb
njG(ξ; x)dξ
(C.18)
2πφD(x) +
ZZ
Sb
φD(ξ)G(ξ; x)
∂nξ
dξ = 4πφ0(x)
(C.19)
where x is a position anywhere on the body surface or outside the body in the ﬂuid domain and
ξ is the source point. Sb denotes, as previously, the wetted surface of the body in its ﬁxed mean
position. G(ξ; x) is the Green function. It describes the potential at a point x due to a point source
located at point ξ with strength −4π. The Green’s function is a source potential,
G(ξ; x) = 1
r + H(ξ; x),
(C.20)
plus a function H that satisﬁes the Laplace equation and boundary conditions, i.e. a free surface
[21, ch. 4.11]. The Green’s function applied in WAMIT is shown in [36, ch. 15-3] for both ﬁnite
and inﬁnite water depth, satisfying the free-surface, radiation conditions and the bottom boundary
condition when relevant.
For the low-order method utilized in this project, the source-only formulation is applied. Thus, the
integral representation of the unknown velocity potential φ(x), takes the form [36, ch.15.4]
φ(x) =
ZZ
Sb
σ(ξ)G(ξ; x) dξ
(C.21)
where G(ξ; x) is the potential at the location ξ due to a source located at the position x. σ(ξ) is the
source strength at position ξ. The ﬂuid velocity components on the body surface is derived from
(C.21) by taking the derivative in the respective direction x = (x, y, z).
XIX
==PAGE:192==
APPENDIX C. WAMIT THEORY
To solve the the integral equations (C.18), (C.19) and (C.21) the body is discretized into ﬂat panels.
The centroid of each panel is the collocation point, i.e source point and origin of normal vectors.
The panels are connected at the vertices which lie directly on the boundary of the body. By
discretization, the solution to the potential, φ, and the source strength, σ is constant over each panel.
However, G(ξ; x) may vary over the panel length.
